               THE ADVENTURES OF A GENTLEMAN IN SEARCH OF MISS SMITH.
            
            BY MISS E. A. DUPUY, Author of "The Conspirator,"
               "Celeste," "The Divorce," "Florence," etc.
            
               
                  DEDICATION. With high respect for the Smith family in general, and the John
                     Smith in particular, the author dedicates to them the following pages. See page
                     33.

            The adventures of a gentleman in search of Miss Smith
            
               THE ADVENTURES OF A GENTLEMAN IN SEARCH OF MISS SMITH.
               
                  CHAPTER I.
                  A fine steamer was dashing gallantly down the Hudson, toward the commercial
                     metropolis of our country. The sun was setting gorgeously, behind the Highlands
                     and the summer wind just rippled the sparkling waters. It was a brilliant
                     evening, and the deck was crowded with groups of passengers, who appeared to
                     enjoy the hour and the scene exceedingly.
                  Among the promenaders was a young man of refined and prepossessing appearance,
                     dressed with extreme care; and his glittering watch-guard, seal-ring, and
                     diamond pin, be trayed a fondness for ornament which might have become one of
                     the opposite sex. He was a slender youth, of dark complex on and black eyes,
                     and there was an expression about the mouth which indicated firmness of
                     purpose, whole the remainder of the features expressed excitability of
                     temperament, and impulsive feeling.
                  At each turn in his walk, the eyes of this young gentleman were irresistably
                     attracted toward a group of three persons who occupied seats near the bow of
                     the boat, or rather toward one member of the party. This was a young lady of
                     slight and girl sh figure, brilliantly fair complexion, and blonde hair. She
                     was not beautiful, but her face was eminently pleasing and attractive, and the
                     bright good-humor, sparkling over it, was more to the taste of her young
                     admirer than perfect beauty of feature would have been.
                  The quiet, elderly people, whom she enlivened by her sprightly sallies, were
                     evidently her parents; and, from the appearance of the three, a stranger would
                     have judged them to be respectable people in comfortable circumstances, who
                     enjoyed the good things of this life, without making a display themselves or
                     caring for that made by others.
                  Young as he evidently was, our hero was an observer, and he accurately divined
                     the position occupied by these persons, who interested him so deeply; for he
                     was already fathoms in love with the young girl, and was endeavoring to find
                     some excuse for approaching the trio, sufficiently near to hazard an address to
                     one of them.
                  Mr. Mortimer Grandval was as romantic a young gentleman as his name could
                     appropriately have been bestowed upon. He was a southerner by birth, and in
                     possession of a handsome, unencumbered property. He had graduated, very
                     recently, at a northern university, and was now making a summer tour,
                     preparatory to his return home in the fall to assume the management of his own
                     affairs. He had strong faith in love at first sight, and he now firmly believed
                     that some intuitive feeling of fitness attracted him so powerfully toward her of the blonde hair and brilliant
                     smile. The Fates destined her to become his, and doubtless they had been
                     providentially thrown together, that the destiny decreed them should be
                     fulfilled.
                  Thus reasoned Mr. Mortimer Grandval, and it seemed as if chance were determined
                     to favor the hypothesis by allowing him an opportunity to gain the desired
                     privilege of addressing her. The young lady arose from her seat to gain a
                     better view of some point in the scenery, and her veil becoming detached from
                     her bonnet, was wafted back among the crowd of passengers. There was a slight
                     scramble between several gentlemen for the possession of it, for Grandval was
                     not the only one attracted by this fresh and artless-looking girl; but our hero
                     proved the fortunate one. With a deferential bow, he approached and presented
                     it to her, at the same time uttering a few words which formed an opening for a
                     conversation.
                  His manner was so respectful, and his appearance so evidently that of a
                     gentleman that, after a slight scrutiny on the part of the parents, they joined
                     in the conversation; thus giving their sanction to the new acquaintance formed
                     by their daughter. Grandval was familiar with the scenery on the river, and he
                     possessed fine natural tact, and agreeable descriptive powers. He could quote
                     poetry, too, with the enthusiasm of a Sophomore, and the brightening eyes and
                     kindling cheeks of his fair listeners betrayed the deepening interest with
                     which they hearkened to his words.
                  When the supper-bell rang, he ventured to offer himself as her escort to the
                     table; and after swallowing a cup of tea at nearly scalding temperature—of
                     which fact, however, he was happily unconscious—he returned with her to the
                     deck, and to the charms of moonlight, sentiment, and music—for there was a fine
                     band on board, which played until a late hour.
                  It was a glorious summer night, and, oh! bliss ineffable! his new acquaintance
                     leaned upon his arm, and promenaded with him, unaccompanied by papa and mamma.
                     Gaining confidence as she became better acquainted, she quoted poetry in her
                     turn, and Grandval discovered that a remarkable sympathy existed in their
                     tastes and feelings. Like most of her young countrywomen, the fair stranger was
                     an enthusiastic admirer of the productions of Willis, and she quoted some of
                     his exquisite fancies in a voice of such clear music as enchanted the
                     listener.
                  "I graduated at the same institution," said Grandval, "and I have often visited
                     spots which were said to be favorite haunts of the poet, and wished that I,
                     too, could gain inspiration from their loveliness. It is the glorious privilege
                     of those who can touch the finer chords of our hearts, by the inspirations of
                     their genius, to be enshrined therein with a species of reverential worship,
                     which is not bestowed upon the author, but on the ideality which clothes in
                     language the silent aspirations of the human soul."
                  "True; and, as I gaze on the scene which lies around us, his words
                     involuntarily occur to me as applicable to this night. In a prose story, which
                     no one else could have written, he describes a scene at Trenton Falls: 'a more
                     omnipresent and radiant atmosphere of moonlight never sprinkled the wilderness
                     with silver.'"
                  "I am sure I can go on and say, with perfect truth, 'it was a night in which to
                     wish it might never be day again.'"
                  "Mary, my love," said the voice of mamma, "I think it is time for us to retire.
                     The air is sensibly cooler, and it grows late."
                  Mary joined her for a moment, and petitioned for only another half hour. "It
                     was a shame to retire to the cabin, and endeavor to sleep, when such a moon was
                     shining over them."
                  The petition was granted, and the two resumed their promenade.
                  When she finally retired, Grandval felt as if the light of even that brilliant
                     night bad departed with her, and he leaned over the balustrade and watched the
                     rippling waters, as the boat cut her swift way through them, and recalled the
                     winning manner and arch smile of his new acquaintance, with the conviction that
                     they must become the charm of his home, or he was a desolate and undone
                     man.
                  The thought suddenly occurred to him that he would find out the name of the
                     parents by applying to the register. She, herself, bore the charmed one of
                     Mary, and all the poetry linked with that sweet appellation came to his mind,
                     and he instantly applied it to his late companion—believing her, in his heart,
                     the brightest and loveliest of all the Marys that have ever lived.
                  He wended his way to the clerk's office, and, after some difficulty, found the
                     name he sought; but never did man gain less information therefrom. He looked at
                     it several moments in blank amazement, for it was so common a designation that
                     it conveyed to him no idea of individuality. John Smith, wife and daughter,
                     stared him in the face. A crowd of John Smiths arose before his mental vision;
                     he had known at least a dozen himself, and as he now endeavored to recall them,
                     he felt assured that he could not recognize one of them if they should suddenly
                     appear before him.
                  John Smith! Absurd, for a man with such a patronymic to inflict upon his son
                     the irreparable injury of bestowing upon him an equally common baptismal
                     appellation! It is a wrong that can not be atoned for; that time will only
                     deepen; for, save the John Smith of Pocahontas celebrity, what
                     trump of Fame has ever bequeathed one of the numerous bearers of that name to
                     the admiration or reverence of posterity? A man with such a cognomen should
                     seek the most extraordinary appellation for his son. For instance, Melchesideck
                     Smith, or Nebuchadnezzar Smith, or Aldibarontapho cophornio Smith, might stand
                     some chance to be remembered, but simple John Smith at once takes his place
                     amid the busy crowd as a nonentity—as one of a numerous legion of brothers
                     bearing the same nondistinctive appellation.
                  Grandval had been so occupied with the daughter that he had neglected to remark
                     the outer man of Mr. Smith with any degree of particularity, but he mentally
                     resolved to do so on the morrow before they parted; for, in a city like New
                     York, where the Smiths are numbered in legions, he might experience some
                     difficulty in finding the real Simon Pure, and on the prosecution of this
                     chance-acquaintance, he fully persuaded himself, depended his future
                     happiness.
                  He retired to his berth after midnight, and tossed on his uneasy pillow until
                     nearly dawn. He then fell into a deep sleep, which lasted several hours. When
                     he awoke, the boat was in port, and, from the sounds which reached his ears, he
                     knew that the passengers were rapidly departing. Should his new acquaintance
                     leave before he again saw them! before he ascertained their place of residence!
                     There was horror in the thought! He did not even know if they lived in New
                     York; they might reside in Louisiana, or at the opposite extremity of the
                     Union, for aught he knew; and the idea of being compelled to search over this
                     wide-spread country for John Smith, with a daughter bearing the equally common
                     name of Mary, was terrible! His pilgrimage might rival that of Evangeline, and
                     end in as unsatisfactory a manner.
                  With trembling haste he performed his toilet, and, without being very precise
                     as to the tie of his cravat, or the curl of his hair, he rushed forth amid the
                     crowd of porters, hackney-coachmen, and others that usually encumber the way. A
                     glance within the open doors of the ladies' cabin assured him that it was quire
                     empty, and he hurried, in great perturbation, toward the clerk's office.
                  "Have all the lady passengers left?" he asked, in quite an excited manner.
                  "Every one, I believe, except a sick lady, who is under the care of the
                     captain. Overslept yourself, eh? Any lady to complain of your neglect?"
                  "No; but I made an acquaintance I am sorry to lose sight of so soon. I wished
                     to ascertain her residence—that is, the residence of her father."
                  "Eh? I know many of the people who were on board; they often travel by this
                     line. Maybe I can help you," said the good natured clerk. "What was the name of
                     the gentleman?"
                  "Smith—John Smith," replied our hero, with a crest-fallen expression.
                  The clerk uttered an expressive whistle.
                  "Sorry I can do nothing for you; but the Smiths are such a numerous family,
                     there is no telling one from the other, 'specially the John Smiths. But there
                     seems to be a party on the wharf yet;
                     perhaps they may prove to be your friends"
                  Grandval rushed out, in a state of great excitement. A girlish figure was
                     springing into a carriage; he thought it must be Mary; another glance at the
                     person of the gentleman who followed her convinced him that his conjecture was
                     correct; he leaped on shore, ran as rapidly as he could for the various
                     impediments which obstructed his way, and came near enough to the vehicle to
                     see that it was occupied by the party he sough—to receive a smile of
                     recognition from the fair Mary—when she was whirled rapidly away, and his cries
                     to the driver to stop were unheard amid the Babel of surrounding sounds.
               
               
                  CHAPTER II.
                  IN a state of complete bewilderment, Grandval made his way on the boat again,
                     and after giving his baggage in charge of a porter, he hurried toward the
                     hallowed spot upon the deck on which he had first beheld his adorable Mary; but
                     he was quite disgusted, and his sentimentality terribly outraged, at finding
                     the place occupied by two stupid-looking children, belonging to the sick lady,
                     who were quarreling over the division of bread and cheese, which one of them
                     held in her soiled apron.
                  Almost execrating these infant innocents, he turned away, and hurried toward a
                     cab in which he was taken, in a most wretched state of mind, to his boarding
                     house, on —— street. It was kept by a widow of highly respectable connections,
                     and with Mrs. Wilson, the quiet manners and gentlemanly appearance of young
                     Grandval had rendered him quite a favorite. She happened to be at the
                     parlor-window when the cab drove to the door, and she noticed his unusual
                     paleness, and the air of wildness which the unsettled state of his feelings
                     unconsciously imparted to him; she therefore came out and received him herself,
                     with almost maternal solicitude.
                  "Glad to see you back again, Mr. Grandval; but, dear me, I hope you have not
                     been ill. You look quite—quite done up, as one may say."
                  "Do I, indeed!" asked Grandval. "No, Mrs. Wilson, I have not been ill, but I
                     have met with a sad disappointment—an adventure which may stamp my life for
                     good or evil—a loss which, if not remedied, may produce fatal
                     consequences."
                  "Dear me, I am sorry to hear that. Pray walk into my own sitting-room, and tell
                     me all about it."
                  The young man felt the necessity of a confidant; his heart was overflowing with
                     disappointment, and he must bestow is fullness on some one; moreover, it
                     occurred to him that Mrs. Wilson's long residence in New York had probably made
                     her well acquainted with many of the tribe of Smiths, more numerous in our day
                     than the children of Israel in their palmiest hour; and she might be able to
                     furnish him a clue to the residence of the veritable John. He therefore
                     followed the good lady into her sanctum, and there related to her the history
                     of the meeting on the steamer, and its consequences, frankly admitting that he
                     had fallen irrevocably in love with the fair Mary at first sight.
                  Mrs. Wilson listened with an air of deep interest, and when he closed his
                     narrative with a sigh, she said,
                  "You need not fall into a state of despondency about this affair, Mr. Grandval,
                     for you may depend upon it Miss Smith will be easily found.
                        I know many Smiths myself, and among them may be the family you
                     seek. Describe this young lady to me; was she beautiful?"
                  "No—O—perhaps not beautiful, but exceedingly attractive; she is sprightly,
                     animated, and evidently good-tempered. He who wishes a "bright spirit for his
                     minister," would prefer her to a more regular beauty."
                  "A charming girl, no doubt; but what is her peculiar style? for I know a Mary
                     Smith, and now I remember about it, her father's name is John.
                     Perhaps she may be your lady-love"
                  Grandval caught at the suggestion eagerly,
                  "If she should prove so, Mrs. Wilson, I shall bless you, indeed. This young
                     lady is very fair, with blue eyes and golden hair."
                  'A tall, delicate figure?" suggested Mrs. Wilson.
                  "Yes—that is, not very tall, but slender and sylph-like."
                  "Hem; the description tallies very well, allowing for the exaggeration of the
                     lover Mary Smith is a nice girl, and a very good housewife, but I never found
                     her quite so charming as you consider her. However, as I am not in love with
                     her, that could not be expected. I can tell you, Mr. Grandval, you have made a
                     more sensible choice than young men usually do; for Miss Smith makes the very
                     best pickles and preserves of any young lady of my acquaintance."
                  "Pickles and preserves," repeated Grandval, with a tragi-comic air. "O, Mrs.
                     Wilson, do not drive me quite beyond the limits of reason, by talking of such
                     every-day affairs, when I feel as if I shall never again be able to eat any
                     thing more than will barely sustain life until I discover the abode of my
                     life's angel."
                  "Pickles will give an appetite sometimes," said the old lady, sententiously,
                     "and preserves are also useful to wrap up pills for those diseased with fevers
                     of any kind, so Mary's skill in that line may prove of some consequence yet,
                     contemptuously as your speak of it. A talent for housekeeping is by no means to
                     be despised, when a man is looking out for a partner for life."
                  The lover groaned in spirit. This was taking a utilitarian view of things which
                     had never occurred to his young romance.
                  "Young men don't often think of these affairs before they rush into matrimony,"
                     continued sensible Mrs. Wilson, "but they very soon find out afterward, if the
                     house is not in order, and the meals not well cooked. Then what a shaking and
                     cuffing poor little Cupid gets! ten chances to one he is so unmercifully
                     berated that he shakes his wings in scorn, and files away forever from those
                     who were so unreasonable as to bring him no assistance in the shape of common
                     sense, to aid him in his efforts to smooth for them the rugged path of life, by
                     shedding the light of his presence over their household."
                  The latter portion of this speech showed that the good lady had something
                     poetic mixed up with her every-day views of life and its duties, and Grandval
                     listened with more respect.
                  "I am glad that I have been so fortunate as blindly to make a choice which so
                     sensible a person as you are can approve," said the young man. "But, dear Mrs.
                     Wilson, if, after all, your Mary Smith and mine should prove not to be the same
                     person—ah, the thought distracts me!"
                  "But I am certain she must be the right one, Mr. Grandval, so you need not take
                     on so. It is scarcely possible that two John Smiths can have a daughter Mary so
                     perfectly alike."
                  "When shall I see her, madam? I am all impatience to ascertain if your
                     conjecture be correct. I would set out this moment if it were possible. Can we
                     not call this morning?"
                  Mrs. Wilson meditated a brief space, and then replied,
                  "I think we had better not be in so great a hurry; because, to tell you the
                     truth, Mrs. Smith is one of the most particular women In the world, and if she
                     is just off a journey, she will be very busy overlooking the house, and finding
                     out how things have gone on in her absence. I can tell you she is'nt one of
                     those mothers who allow their daughters to sit in the parlor entertaining
                     company while they are busy about household matters; no, Mary goes heart and
                     hand with her mother in everything she undertakes. Ah, Mr. Grandval, you will
                     obtain a jewel of a wife."
                  "I am glad to hear it, Mrs. Wilson; but pray, when will it be proper to call? I
                     shall not sleep until I ascertain the identity of your young friend with my new
                     acquaintance."
                  "Well, as I've known these Smiths a long time, though I can't say I'm very
                     intimate with them, I think I may venture to take you there this evening;
                     'specially as you are not an entire stranger, and a young man like yourself,
                     who comes as a lover, is always graciously received."
                  Thus it was arranged, and Grandval retired to his room with a mind a little
                     more at ease. He passed the day in looking over poetic extracts from which to
                     call the most beautiful quotations for the evening's conversation, varying his
                     employment by occasiona visits to the
                     mirrror, to ascertain if he was in a good looks as usual.
                  He had little appetite for his dinner, which Mrs. Wilson observed, and
                     recommended to him some very fine pickles as a provocative, remarking, at the
                     same time, that they were nearly as good as those manufactured by a certain
                     fair friend of theirs, who should be nameless. Grandval blushed, and the other
                     boarders looked at him as if he were some newly imported phenomenon of the
                     animal species, in place of that very common specimen of the genus homo, a
                     young gentleman very decidedly and verdantly in love.
                  Evening at last came, and after a most elaborate toilet, our lover joined Mrs.
                     Wilson in such perturbation of spirits as was quite pitiable. He was evidently
                     in such a tremulous state that she deeply sympathised with him, and made many
                     efforts to direct his thoughts by conversing on such themes as she fancied
                     would possess an interest for him; but she might as well have addressed her
                     remarks to the man in the moon as to the earthly mortal who walked by her side
                     in a state of nervous abstraction which rendered him oblivious of every thing
                     that was passing around him.
                  After walking about eight squares, Mrs. Wilson announced that the end of their
                     pilgrimage had arrived. Grandval looked up; they were in front of a red brick
                     house which made one of a long row, all of which were exactly alike, and looked
                     as if they were built for people who were all formed on the same pattern. It is
                     difficult to fancy any individuality character among persons content to live in
                     a brick row, and yet, if we could indeed play the part of the Diable Boiteux, we should find tragedy and comedy,
                     happiness and misery, crime and integrity, dwelling side by side in those dull
                     uniform looking walls.
                  To Grandval's eyes, the re brick was a temple which enshrined his divinity, and
                     he felt almost vexed with his good friend when she called his attention to the
                     glitter of the gaslights on the brass knobs upon the door.
                  "It is easy to see that this is the neatest, house in the row, Mr. Grandval.
                     See, there is not another door handle half so brilliant all the way down."
                  The ring at the bell was promptly answered by a half grown girl, who looked as
                     if shining from the recent application of soap and water; and her hair was
                     smoothed on her round head as flatly as if it had been glued
                     down. Her dress was very short, and rather narrow, but spotlessly clean.
                  A gas-light burned in the hall, and a lamp with a summer-shade stood in the
                     front parlor. Grandval knew not why, but the aspect of this room struck a chill
                     through him, even on this summer night. It was so formal—so precisely neat in
                     every respect, that it seemed impossible for any genial flow of soul ever to
                     take place within its walls. The chairs were all covered with brown linen, and
                     stood marshalled in stiff array around the room; this-sombre drapery invested
                     every thing, and seemed to cast a "brown horror" over the apartment.
                  It is true that vases of flowers stood on the marble mantle, but they were made
                     of shells, and the wiry stems stood out in regular order around the parent
                     stalk as stiff as the quills on a porcupine. No artificial imitation of nature
                     which has yet been attempted is so uninteresting to the imagination as shell
                     flowers. Hard, stiff, scentless things, they excite surprise by their
                     ingenuity, but no sentiment can possibly be attached to them; and Grandval, who
                     thought the treasures of Flora the only fitting offering for one so young and
                     fair as his Mary, turned with disgust from the glittering counterfeits of
                     nature's fairest daughters.
                  Several handsomely bound volumes were placed in orderly array upon a table, and
                     while awaiting Miss Smith's appearance, Grandval ventured to take one up. It
                     opened of itself to a receipt for making preserved ginger, and to his surprise
                     he found that a copy of Miss Leslie's cookery book had found its way into the
                     parlor. Curiosity now induced him to look over the other volumes, and to his
                     increased astonishment he found them of the same character; they were all works
                     on gastronomy and the art of living well.
                  Mrs. Wilson remarked his surprise, and glancing at them, she said,
                  "Ah, Mr. Smith is . He is a bon
                        vivant of the highest type, and thinks that people only live to
                     eat. He says that those who teach us something new in the art of cookery
                     deserve the highest place of honor; so he has had his favorite authors in that
                     line elegantly bound, and promoted them to a place in the parlor."
                  "A singular fancy, I must confess," commenced Grandval, but his words died on
                     his lips, and he felt as if he would have been much obliged to the insensible
                     floor if it would have taken pity on his confusion at that instant, opened wide
                     its wooden jaws, and have allowed him to pass into the depths of darkness
                     below. In the open doorway just in front of him appeared a young lady, toward
                     whom Mrs. Wilson advanced with alacrity, at the same time addressing her as her
                     dear Mary thus leaving no gleam of hope in the stunned soul of Mr. Mortimer
                     Grandval—for, O, terrible to relate! the person who thus appeared was not
                        the Miss Smith. The resemblance which had deluded him into the
                     belief that it might be his Mary, was just enough to warrant the supposition,
                     while at the same time it was an insult to the fresh and blooming image
                     enshrined in his heart, to compare it with the hard-looking, light-haired girl
                     who was now introduced to him as Miss Mary Smith.
                  The young lady bowed stiffly and coldly, and seemed evidently surprised at his
                     perturbation. Mrs. Wilson regarded it as only the bashfulness of a young lover,
                     and while the cold perspiration was bursting from his brow, she went on with
                     great volubility to say that at the request of her late traveling companion,
                     she had brought him to pay his respects; as he hoped to be permitted to
                     cultivate an acquaintance so auspiciously begun.
                  The lady turned her light blue eyes on the shivering form of the abashed young
                     man, and he felt as if they might have been little pellets of ice with a small
                     spot of paly blue in the center of each, so chilling was the expression they
                     wore, "Excuse me, Mrs. Wilson, but I am entirely at a loss to understand you,"
                     she said in a voice as dry and harsh as her form was bony. "I have not been on
                     a journey this summer, though we contemplate making one. In fact, we should
                     have gone before this time, but just as we were ready to leave, the discovery
                     was made, that the household stores prepared by ourselves,
                     had suffered from the great heat of the weather, and many of them were in a
                     state of fermentation. Under these circumstances, it was impossible to leave
                     before the mischief was remedied."
                  Grandval could have groaned audibly, but he courageously pent up his feelings
                     within his own breast, and forced himself to say,
                  "I beg your pardon, Miss Smith, but the similarity in your name, has led me
                     into a strange mistake. I made the acquaintance of Miss Mary Smith on the North
                     River yesterday, and as I neglected to ascertain her place of residence, I
                     ventured to call with my good friend, Mrs. Wilson, hoping you would prove to be
                     the lady."
                  Miss Smith grew more stately every moment as this explanation proceeded, and
                     her eyes appeared to become colder and colder, until little points of ice
                     seemed actually to radiate from them. With great stateliness she said,
                  "My name, sir, is not Smith, but Smythe, as you might easily have
                     ascertained, if you had consulted the directory. I should be extremely sorry to
                     be confounded with the vulgar tribe of Smiths, whose ancestors must have been
                     either gun makers, or horse shoe manufactures; as in ancient times people are
                     said to have derived their names from the handicraft they followed, or from
                     some estate,"
                  Grandval had a great inclination to inquire of her how her
                     ancestors obtained theirs, but he prudently refrained, and relapsed into a
                     state of wretched recklessness, while the two ladies kept up a constrained
                     conversation, in which he made not the slightest effort to join. Miss Smythe
                     had frozen him up completely, and as he looked at her he wondered that Mrs.
                     Wilson had not vaunted her powers of making ice-cream, as well as pickles;
                     though he mentally acknowledged that she need only look into a jar of the
                     latter, and the highest point of acidity would be attained.
                  In a state of completed obliviousness as to what was passing around him,
                     Grandval sat a wretched half hour with the murmur of conversation ringing in
                     his ears, and then mechanically obeyed the motion of Mrs. Wilso to leave. With a profound bow, and a few
                     muttered words, he bade adieu to Miss Smythe with the devout hope that he
                     should never behold her fair face again.
                  "Dear me, who would have believed that such a blunder could be made!"
                     ejaculated Mrs. Wilson when they were fairly outside of the house. "And the
                     description you gave applied so well to this young lady too!"
                  "Don't say that Mrs. Wilson if you please," said our hero in a state of
                     exasperation. "I never could have said any thing that could lead you to suppose
                     me quite dull enough to fall in love with such a mummy as that girl Why she is
                     an absolute fright! I told you my Mary is a sylph, and you seem to
                     think she is as bony as Miss Fox."
                  "I'm not acquainted with that lady," said Mrs. Wilson who was guiltless of
                     literary airs, "but I am sure you said she is fair and golden-haired, and so is
                     this young lady. Besides, you said she is not beautiful, and I am sure neither
                     is Miss Smythe."
                  "No, by the Mass! a more lack-lustre imitation of humanity I hope never to see.
                     She surely does not inherit the paternal fondness for the good things of this
                     life, or she would, scarcely be as attenuated as she is."
                  "I can not say as to that, but he certainly excels in preparing them, and I am
                     sorry you missed the chance of such a pattern-wife as she will make."
                  "My dear Mrs. Wilson, such a regret would almost prove you to be no friend to
                     my happiness. If such a woman could win me, I would give her leave to bestow
                     her choicest acid upon me, as she does on her boasted pickles."
                  We leave the reader to imagine the state of mind in which the disappointed
                     Grandval spent the night. We can not assert that the did not even tear his
                     hair, as he perambulated his floor through its long hours, and thought of his
                     terrible disappointment. The image of the true Mary Smith arose before him more
                     charming than ever, when he contrasted her with the counterfeit he had been
                     deceived into seeking.
               
               
                  CHAPTER III.
                  THE apartment occupied by our hero joined that of another single gentleman, on
                     whom Mrs. Wilson's allusion at dinner that day had not been lost, as, in fact
                     few things were, for Mr. Medler possessed the most insatiable curiosity, and an
                     extreme fondness for assisting his friends out of difficulties; which
                     assistance frequently tended only to plunge them deeper into
                     what they were seeking to extricate themselves from.
                  Mr. Medler was by several years the senior of Grandval, and possessed the
                     further advantage of being city bred, so that he considered himself up to any
                     thing. The distracted perambulations of his neighbor were distinctly audible to
                     him through the thin partition which divided their room, and
                     he mentally decided to find out what it was all about before the following day
                     was over.
                  In pursuance of his plan, he knocked at Grandval's door as soon as he was
                     dressed, there by arousing that unhappy wight from a light slumber into which
                     he had fallen about daylight.
                  "Who is there?" he peevishly demanded.
                  "O, you're alive are you?" replied the voice of his fellow-boarder, "May I Come
                     in?"
                  "Yes, if you wish it; the door is not locked."
                  Medler entered and found Grandval lying on the outside of the bed in the same
                     clothes he had worn on the preceding evening, with the exception of his coat,
                     which his excited promenade had rendered inconveniently warm, and it had
                     therefore been laid aside. He looked pale, and the livid circles beneath his
                     eyes showed that he really suffered.
                  "My dear fellow," said Medler. 'I heard you moving about all night, and feared
                     you were ill, but I would'nt intrude then, because I thought you would call our
                     if you needed assistance. What's in the wind, my boy? Been losing more money
                     than you can conveniently pay?"
                  "I never gamble; it is a worse loss than money," he feebly said.
                  "Ha, well, that is according to one's own estimate of things Now to me money is
                     the representative of every thing in the shape of human felicity; therefore I
                     estimate its loss above every other."
                  "There are many things it can not buy nevertheless."
                  "As a lost heart for instance," suggested Medler determined to hazard a bold
                     stroke. "Any body can see with half an eye, that you have fallen over head in
                     love; come isn't it so now?"
                  Grandval sighed heavily.
                  "If I could only find the girl I am in love with, I shouldn't care," he
                     said.
                  "What, parents opposed? sent her out of the way? The deuce! More romantic than
                     agreeable that!"
                  "No, that is not it. I have lost all clue to her, through my own want of
                     forethought."
                  "Pooh! that is easily remedied. Apply to the director—find
                     out her father's residence—I will scrape up an acquaintance, and
                     introduce you all regular."
                  "But, but, the name is the difficulty.
                  "You don't mean to say that you fell in love with the girl to the point you've
                     reached, and did not find out her name?"
                  "No, I know her name, certainly, but that does not help me much."
                  "What is it then, in Heaven's name?"
                  "Mary Smith."
                  Mr. Medler looked nonplussed a moment but he brightened up and briskly
                     said.
                  "Well, it her father's name isn't John, we can manage matters yet?"
                  "But unfortunately her father's name is John," said the lover
                     despairingly, and the friend sat still, and hummed a tune, in a state of
                     despondency. Presently he slowly said,
                  "It never was asserted that Jack Medler was not ready with
                     an expedient for any mischance. My advice is, to call a convention of all the
                     John Smiths in the city, and then pick out your man."
                  "But I only met them on a steamboat. I do not remember the old gentleman; it
                     was the daughter I fell in love with, and as I had no intention of so soon
                     losing sight of them, I scarcely noticed the appearance of the parents."
                  The adviser again seemed puzzled.
                  "Ah, but that is bad; but couldn't you ask each one in the assembly, if he has
                     a daughter Mary, who is the most charming girl in the city?"
                  "And perhaps half the men present, would reply in the affirmative; for Mary is
                     unfortunately as common a designation as Smith, and fathers often think their
                     daughters mere charming than any one else considers them; no that will never
                     do."
                  "Ah, well, perhaps not. Tell me the whole story, and it maybe I can help you. I
                     have been in the city all my life, and know a pretty considerable number of the
                     Smith family myself. At all events, I will do my best to assist you in your
                     search."
                  "Thank you, Medler, I feel the need of some one to advise with, and I know you
                     are sincere in your offer."
                  "You may say that my good fellow. I have a zeal in my friend's service, if I
                     sometimes lack discretion. But let me hear this heart history, if you
                     please."
                  Glad to find a sympathizing listener, Grandval related the story of his
                     unfortunate case up to the close of the last evening, and Medler chuckled over
                     the description of Miss Smythe, and the chilling process he had undergone.
                  "Know those persons myself, dined there once on turtle and venison; fine
                     dinner, very, but a tun a wine wouldn't warm the mother and daughter, while the
                     father is jolly as Bacchus. They are aristocratic, and only tolerate our good
                     landlady because she happens to be connected with some of the upper ten; though
                     what advantage the relationship is, no body can see, for her rich cousins do
                     nothing for her in her reverses, poor soul. If Miss Smythe had known you were
                     from the south, of good family, and owner of a fair share of cotton bales, she
                     might have thawed a little."
                  "Do not talk of her; the recollection of her is odious to me. Rather let us
                     decide on the best course for discovering my own Mary."
                  "Ah, true, Mary is a sweet name, but I could wish that in the present instance
                     the lady bore one a little less common. Now it is by no means usual for ladies
                     to bear the names of our sex, but it is sometimes done, and if this young girl
                     were only Miss Jemmy Smith, we might
                     soon overcome the difficulty. However, we can but set our wits to work, and it
                     shall never be said that Jack Medler was baffled in what he undertook to
                     perform."
                  We have said that Mr. Medler was fond of assisting his friends; in fact, he was
                     quite a philanthropist in his way, the only misfortune being that the aid he
                     rendered was often of exceedingly doubtful advantage to the recipient. While
                     Grandval confided his case to him, a scheme floated through his brain by which
                     he thought two friends might be benefitted at one stroke, if he could only
                     bring the plan to bear.
                  "This young fellow is green," thought the city man, "and easily caught by a
                     pretty face He has never seen this girl but once, and why shall not any other
                     Miss Smith answer as well, even if her name is not Mary? My pretty
                     cousin Kitty Smith, is as charming as this young lady, no doubt, and I know she
                     will be glad to make a good match. If I can bring it to pass, old Jeremiah
                     would accommodate me with that other five hundred as a reward for ridding him
                     of the eldest of six girls. Kate is a dashing girl, and a few smiles and
                     sentimental speeches will soon win this youngster's heart from the other one;
                     then hey presto for a merry weddin."
                  The last thought inspired him with such gleeful anticipations, that he snapped
                     his fingers, and commenced a species of tattoo with his feet which caused his
                     young friend to stare at him with astonishment.
                  "What is the matter?" he asked.
                  "Matter? O, nothing," replied Mr. Medler, in some confusion, "only you see I
                     was thinking that we will soon be on the right track. I just happen to remember
                     a Miss Smith who must be the one you are in pursuit of, and I'll tell you the
                     reason why I think it must be the same. This young lady is a distant relative
                     of my own, and I know certainly that she visited Albany last week in company
                     with her parents, and returned yesterday morning."
                  This was a pure invention on the part of Mr. Medler, but it answered the
                     purpose he wished, for Grandval was eager to believe him and readily permitted
                     himself to be duped. He started up, exclaimin.
                  "My dear friend, you give me new life! Why did I not apply to you at once?
                     Shall we seek her to-day? I am all impatience to behold her again."
                  "Softly, softly, my dear boy, I understand all that, and will set about
                     obtaining an introduction for you as speedily as possible, but these things
                     must be managed with care sometimes. The fact is, old Je—that is my respectable
                     cousin John Smith—belongs to the class of particulars, and he does not permit
                     every one to visit his daughter in the character of a suitor, who may happen to
                     take a fancy to her on a steamboat. I must see him, tell him who you are, and
                     request permission to introduce you into his family circle."
                  As this sounded very plausible and highly respectable, too, Grandval acquiesced
                     in the necessity, and after a few more questions which were satisfactorily
                     answered by Medler, Grandval arose in better spirits, and proceeded to make
                     himself look less like a wanderer from the world of unrest, and more like a
                     young gentleman of the nineteenth century, who was fully conscious of a fair
                     portion of good looks.
                  Mrs Wilson was happy to see Mr. Grandval better than she had expected, after
                     the adventure of the preceding evening, and she was quite electrified in the
                     course of the morning, when he entered her room, and jested quite merrily on
                     the mistake they had made.
                  "I am glad to see you are getting over it, Mr. Grandval You don't seem half so
                     heart struck as you were yesterday."
                  "Perhaps Miss Smythe froze me so effectually last night that I have no warmth
                     left in me. Oh, no, Mrs. Wilson, I have not forgotten my lady-love, nor proved
                     false to her so soon, I assure you; but I have a strong hope—almost a
                     certainty—of seeing her before twenty-four hours roll by."
                  Ah! Well I congratulate you; but how was the discovery of her residence brought
                     about?"
                  "By the simplest means in the world. I have taken, one of my follow-boarders
                     into my confidence, and he happens to know the family."
                  "How does he know it is the same one He may only lead you into a worse scrape
                     than I did."
                  "Oh, several circumstances convince me that there can be no mistake in the
                     present instance. Medler is too keen to be deceived in a matter of the
                     kind."
                  "I don't know; men are not often more sagacious than women, in such affairs,
                     and I was mistaken. Besides, Mr. Medler is notorious for getting
                     others into difficulties; he has the best intentions in the world, but always
                     acts with the greatest want of judgment. I am sorry he happens to be your
                     adviser."
                  "Set your fears at rest dear Madame; I am certain that we are on the right
                     track this time. In fact, the young lady is a relation of his own. Singular
                     that it should turn out so, but fact is sometimes stranger than fiction."
                  In the meantime, Mr. Medler proceeded toward one of the most genteel portions
                     of the city, and rang at the door of a house, struccoed and painted in
                     imitation of stone, with pilasters and columns in front, and a small green
                     yard, in which vegetated a few stunted and miserable-looking evergreens. This
                     was evidently the abode of pretension, and Mr. Medler smiled as he glanced into
                     the half open hall-door, and saw a young lady dressed in a very loose and
                     rather soiled morning-wrapper, with her hair still in curl-papers. She caught a
                     glimpse of his figure, and was about to vanish, when he called to her—
                  "Never mind, Kit, it is only I, and I wish to see you particularly."
                  With a hop, skip, and jump, and young girl thus addressed approached him, and
                     held our her hand.
                  "So it's only you, sure enough, Cousin Jack and pray what
                     brings you here so early this morning? See, I have not even had time to dress
                     my hair, you horrid Vandal. But you are always taking one by surprise."
                  "Something is sometimes gained in that way, Coz. For
                     instance, if I had fallen in love with your hyacinthine locks, last evening, I
                     should have been quite disenchanted this morning, at finding them converted
                     into horned snakes that might rival those of Medusa."
                  The girl laughed, and put up her hands toward the long rolls of paper around
                     which her curls were twisted, as if endeavoring to shelter them from
                     observation.
                  "Never mind, Kate, I am not in love with you, nor likely to become so, as you
                     know very well. Let us go into the boudoir, as I believe you call it, and have
                     a sensible talk. I know you can be rational sometimes."
                  "Thank you; and I also know that you can be complimentary sometimes, cousin of
                     mine. Come in, and let me hear what your wisdom has to say."
                  She led the way into a small apartment opening from the drawing-room, which was
                     ornamented with pictures and statues in abundance; but amid the redundancy of
                     ornament, the lack of good taste was at once apparent. Every thing was
                     overdone.
                  The young lady threw herself into the 
                        cor-
                        corner
                     
                     vis a vis, luxuriously cushioned, and
                     motioned to her visitor to take the opposite seat. Medler obeyed, and then
                     inquired—
                  "Well, Kate, how do you and young Simons get along? Has he asked the momentous
                     question yet?"
                  Miss Smith pouted, and, after a pause, said—
                  "What can one make of such a stupidly-bashful man as he is? I know he is in
                     love with me, but I do not believe he will ever have sufficient self-possession
                     to ask any woman to marry him."
                  "Ah, so much the better just now, for I was afraid you were entangled there,
                     and my present views for you might be thwarted."
                  "What do you mean, Jack?"
                  "Simply that if young Simmons is a good match, I can almost insure you a much
                     better one, by a little tact and management on your part."
                  The girl listened attentively, and, after a moment's reflection, replied—
                  "You know that my position at home is not happy, Cousin, for we are a house
                     divided against itself, and one and all devoted to that most uncomfortable way
                     of living—mere outside show. You also know that every day I am reminded that I
                     am the eldest of six daughters, and nearly twenty, without having yet had a
                     serious offer. Under the circumstances, no one can wonder that I wish to marry
                     some good man with whom I can live respectably Now who is this new lover, and what part am I to
                     play to win him?"
                  "He is a handsome young man, about two years your senior; independent in
                     fortune, and of good manners. All that is requisite is for you to put another
                     Miss Smith out of his romantic head, and usurp her place yourself."
                  "If he is already in love, it seems to me an idle speculation on your part, and
                     a waste of smiles and graces on mine."
                  "Not at all. He has never seen the girl but once, and that on a steamboat; they
                     walked in the moonlight, and talked sentiment until he lost his wits
                     completely. You can get up a very pretty flirtation when you have mind to, and
                     I advise you to try your powers on him. As clever a girl as you are can surely
                     manage this verdant young chap."
                  "But tell me who he is—every thing you know about him—that I may judge of the
                     expediency of taking the trouble."
                  Mr. Medler forth with related the adventures of Grandval, at which Miss Kate
                     laughed merrily, but she became a little serious when she ascertained that he
                     was really well educated, independent, and free to act for himself. Their
                     consultation ended by permission being accorded to introduce the young
                     gentleman that evening.
                  Mr. Medler returned to his friend in high glee; but fearful of being questioned
                     too closely, he took care to dine out, and only to arrive at Mrs. Wilson's late
                     in the evening. Although he was not at all scrupulous on the score of truth, he
                     thought it as well to avoid departing from it as far as possible in the present
                     instance, as no one could foresee the 
                        denonément
                        denouément
                      of the plot he had undertaken to conduct. To Grandval's look of eager
                     inquiry, he replied,
                  "All right, my good fellow. I have smoothed the way for your introduction, and
                     the fair Smith will receive you this evening with the full consent of Pa and
                     Ma, and even including that of brother Jonas."
                  "She has a brother, then?"
                  "Lots of 'em—two older than herself, and two younger, besides five junior
                     sisters."
                  "Quite an extensive family," said Grandval; "but if they are all as charming as
                     Mary, their home must be a heaven on earth."
                  "Then—O, yes—very blissful, indeed. Excellent people, the Smith's are; none
                     better; but, my dear fellow, it is time to make your toilette," so saying, he
                     passed hurriedly into his room, leaving his young dupe in high spirits to
                     prepare for the intended visit.
                  Dressed to the highest point of the becoming, Grandval entered the cab of
                     Medler, and with a quickly-beating heart, was driven toward the mansion of Mr.
                     Jeremiah Smith. The hall and parlors were brilliantly lighted and excited as he
                     was, Grandval remarked the showy appearance of every thing, and he was not as
                     favorably impressed as he had expected to be by the home of the quiet looking
                     people he had met on the steamer.
                  In the front parlor was a young gentleman of tall figure, sandy hair, and
                     parchment complexion, who was introduced as Mr. Jonas Smith. An elderly lady,
                     who strikingly resembled her son, sat on a sofa, and Grandval bowed to her in a
                     species of be wilderment, for it seemed impossible that this bedizened figure
                     could be the same matronly woman in black, whom he had lately traveled
                     with.
                  Mrs. Smith wore her light hair or wig in short spiral curls that hung down in
                     an even files to her eyebrows, and were not even divided in the center. Beneath
                     these peered a pair of small, sharp looking eyes, whose expression was not
                     softened by the remainder of the features. Above this array of curls, which
                     looked more like little wisps of dried hay than any thing else, was placed an
                     elaborate cap, composed of flowers and small bits of blonde. The artificial
                     tinting of the cheeks and lips completed a toute
                        ensemble any thing but pleasing to the visitor.
                  With a painful contraction of the heart, Grandval bowed before her, and
                     received her affected salutation. He found means to whisper to Medler.
                  "Get me out of this, Jack, for we're in the wrong box again. I never saw this
                     lady before."
                  "Too late now, old fellow, you must spend the evening, at all events," returned
                     Medler in the same tone. "Wait till the daughter comes in—she is a regular
                     smasher, and no mistake."
                  Grandval sank into a seat, feeling himself a victim to the most untoward of all
                     circumstances, while Mrs. Smith arranged the lace around her hands, spread out
                     her fan, and prepared herself for conversation.
                  "A delightful day we have enjoyed, Mr. Grandly, I am sure, as I said to my
                     daughter this morning, It'ly could'nt produce finer sun-shine than we walked in
                     to day."
                  Before Grandval could reply, her son gruffly said,
                  "What else would you expect but hot sunshine at this season, ma'am? I dare say
                     Mr. Grandval has been sufficiently melted in it to appreciate all its heating
                     qualities. Foreigners say that we always begin a conversation by speaking of
                     the weather, which is very true. Since I saw the observation, I make it a point
                     never to do it. One American, at least, will repudiate the custom.
                     How is cotton, Mr. Grandval?"
                  If he had asked the price of the horned horse or any other physical phenomenon,
                     he might just as soon have expected a satisfactory reply from our young
                     gentleman, for he had not yet entered on the management of his own property,
                     and only cared to know that he derived from it an income more than sufficient
                     for his most extravagant wants. He simply replied,
                  "I do not know; I have never interested mysel in commercial affairs. I believe
                     the poet's corner is more frequently consulted by me than the prices
                     current."
                  "Ah-h-h—not a business man, I perceive. As a grower of the staple, I thought
                     you would naturally take an interest in its value."
                  "In years to come, I may do so, but hitherto my property has been under the
                     management of an uncle, who is an experienced planter. Since my majority, I
                     have been absent from home, and have had no hing to do but receive the income
                     and appropriate it as suits myself."
                  Mrs. Smith listened, and her small eyes sparkled.
                  "What a fortunate thing it will be for Kate if she can only win him," she
                     thought; and Mr. Jonas mentally determined that his visit to their house should
                     not be made without some result.
                  At that instant a slight rustling noise was heard near the door, and a young
                     lady dressed in white appeared. Grandval, at the first glimpse was struck with
                     her showy appearance. Her figure was tall and slightly inclined to embonpoint, and her complexion brilliantly fair,
                     while her eyes were large, dark and languishing. Her black hair hung in long
                     ringlets in front, and her rosy lips unclosed on a set of white and regular
                     teeth.
                  Altogether, Miss Smith, if not the one sought, was a very pleasing looking
                     girl, and by no means an objectionable acquaintance, thought Grandval. As she
                     advanced into the room she made a little theatrical start as her eye fell on
                     our hero, and uttered a slight exclamation. Medler hastened to introduce his
                     friend, and then the young lady blushed with the most charming confusion, and
                     said,
                  "I was under the impression that I was to meet an acquaintance in Mr. Grandval.
                     My cousin led me to believe that I should see in him one I have already had the
                     pleasure of meeting while traveling."
                  "Travelling! when and where, I wonder," muttered Jonas. "To my certain
                     knowledge, your travels have not extended beyond the limits of the city for the
                     last year."
                  Grandval arose, made his most polite bow, and expressed his regrets that Miss
                     Smith should have been disappointed in not recognizing an acquaintance in
                     himself; that, however, could soon be remedied; he hoped they would soon become
                     friends.
                  He felt himself wounderfully at his ease, and was soon engaged in quite a
                     flirtation with Miss Kate, who used her eyes and her tongue with equal effect.
                     Our hero was not quite so inexperienced as Mr. Medler imagined. He had been
                     accustomed to ladies society during his sojourn at the University, and was able
                     to talk sentiment and gallantry with his new acquaintance in a manner that both
                     astonished and charmed her. From her cousin's description, she had expected to
                     see quite a raw youth, and she was agreeably disappointed to find her powers
                     lavished on one so well worthy of their exercise.
                  Medler judiciously devoted himself to the entertainment of the Mama, who, on being snubbed
                     by her son at the commencement of the interview, had subsided into a state of
                     silent indignation, from which Cousin Jack's efforts at last aroused her.
                     Common-places were uttered, and the faded curls and brilliant artificials
                     shaken in concert with them until Cousin Jack thought his five hundred hardly
                     earned, even if he eventually succeeded in loosening the purse-strings of the
                     father of the family.
                  Mr. Jonas amused himself with making calculations with a pencil in a small book
                     with a yellow leather cover, which he drew from his pocket. If the guest had
                     been privileged to peep into these memoranda, he would have found calculations
                     of the commissions due for selling cottons at various prices, which, in the
                     imagination of the writer, were already as good as in the possession of the
                     firm of Jeremiah Smith & Son.
                  In the meantime, Miss Smith inveigled Grandval into the boudoir under the
                     pretext of hearing music by moonlight. A French window opened from this room on
                     a balcony surrounded by iron railings, and ornamented with a stand of dusty
                     flowers. In this two chairs were placed, and Miss Kate tuned her guitar in a
                     patch of moonlight that fell between two neighboring chimney-tops, and was
                     sufficient to light up her instrument and her lilly hands, while the remainder
                     of her person rested in the shadowy star-lit air.
                  She sang passably, and talked very well; Grandval was amused and interested,
                     but Miss Smith was mistaken in fancying that she was making a lasting
                     impression upon his heart. That was irrevocably the property of
                     the fair snow drop, whose image he cherished only the more fondly from the
                     disappointments he encountered in finding her. He had tact
                     enough to understand Miss Smith at a glance, and was quite willing to indulge
                     her fondness for flirtation; never dreaming that any thing serious could be
                     expected to grow out of this chance visit to her family.
                  "This is indeed a lovely night!" exclaimed the young lady, casting up her eyes
                     in affected ecstacy.
                  
                  "Lovely is the true epithet, Miss Smith, for I think it was made to talk love
                     in," replied Grandval, with theatrical emphasis, as he stood in a graceful
                     attitude before her. "I could make the hackneyed quotation— 
                        "On such a night as this young Jessica," etc.
                      but I forbear, as it must be well known to you, and I would not repeat
                     what has become common place in such a presence, or in so poetic a scene as the
                     one before me. It might inspire even my dull wit with such thoughts as the
                     world does not willingly lt die."
                  "I am sure you are a poet, Mr. Grandval. You talk like one, and then you are
                     from the land of the orange and the vine. I really thing that the very sight of
                     an orange grove would inspire me with the divine afflatus."
                  "Unluckily I am a little too far north for the cultivation of the orange, and
                     the view that oftenest rises up before my mental vision, is a field of dead
                     cotton stalks, raising their brown and shrivelled arms toward heaven in every
                     degree of crookedness that can be imagined, as if imploring the removal of
                     their unsightliness from the fair face of nature."
                  "O! you horror! you destroyer of one's ideal pictures!" exclaimed the young
                     lady. "I have imagined a palace-like building raising its stately front amid
                     the green foliage and snowy blossoms of a lovely orange grove, on which cluster
                     the ripened fruit and the fragrant flowers at the same time A gleam of silvery
                     waters through a wide park on which stand noble forest trees, the growth of
                     centuries, completes the picture."
                  "The homes of many of the very wealthy a little nearer the tropics, may realize
                     your fancy sketch; but, in my state, by far the larger portion of the planters
                     belong to the juste milieu, and with them
                     comfort outweighs ostentatious show. Imagine a cottage building surrounded by
                     wide galleries, and shaded by trees sufficiently trimmed to allow a free
                     circulation of air; a neat fence, or an ever-green hedge forming the boundary
                     to a yard often large enough to be dignified with the name of lawn, if the
                     owner is ambitious. About a quarter of a mile off, is seen a small village,
                     consisting of two parallel rows of houses with small gardens attached to them
                     and often a narrow enclosure in front, in which a fruit tree is permitted to
                     grow, provided the owners will not devour the fruit before it ripens."
                  "It is a fair picture, but then the producers are——" she hesitated, as if
                     unwilling to wound him by uttering the thought that was in her mind.
                  "Slaves, you would say," continued Grandval. "That is true, and to be lamented,
                     but since we must have them in our midst, we must do the best we can with them;
                     and those orderly, well-cared for blacks, are in a very superior condition, I
                     assure you, not only to many of their own color but to hundreds of their white
                     brethren to be found in this very city. Within an hour's walk from this
                     pleasant home of yours, I can find scenes that humanity must weep and shudder
                     over. Let the philanthropist enter those and purge them, and then let him say
                     to his southern brother, 'thou committest a wrong against thy fellow man by
                     holding him in bondage.' Armed with the evidence of his far-reaching
                     benevolence, which had swept crime and suffering from his own door, the
                     slave-holder would meet him in the spirit of kindliness, and the means of
                     ridding our country of this curse be jointly discussed in the spirit of anmity
                     which should exist between the sons of this vast heritage, and not, as now, be
                     sounded as the tocsin of political warfare. But pardon me, Miss Smith, we have
                     diverged widely from the orthodox topics usually discussed by young ladies and
                     gentlemen, on occasions like the present. Will you sing for me again?"
                  The tinkle of the guitar arose, and Miss Smith caroled a gay air, after which
                     the sound of a voice near to Grandval informed him that the party in the
                     drawing-room was increased by another arrival.
                  "It is mon cher papa," said his fair companion, starting up with an affected
                     vivacity. "He will never forgive me if I delay presenting so agreeable an
                     acquaintance as yourself to him. Allons—let
                     us abjure moonlight for lamplight and the pleasures of the social circle."
                  Grandval followed her through the boudoir, and was introduced to an elderly
                     gentleman, who was very bald and very white; he looked as if he had spent all
                     the days of his life in the shades of his counting house, with no gleam of
                     physical or mental sunshine to play upon his outward or inner man. It was
                     evident that Mr. Smith was a hard, dry man of ledgers, whose ideas seldom
                     soared beyond cent per cent. and the thought suggested itself to Grandval, that
                     the over-dressed woman in the hay-colored wig, must have been the
                     representative of a certain portion of real estate, or her mercantile partner
                     would never have selected her as his companion in the voyage of life. It solved
                     a doubt in his mind as to Mr. Smith's entire sanity when he made the choice, as
                     he had been mentally accusing that respectable individual of remarkable want of
                     taste in his judgment of the fairer part of creation. As to himself, he
                     regarded Mrs. Smith as a frightful specimen of the sex, whom it would be a
                     penance to be even distantly connected with, and wondered how so passable a
                     girl as Kate could be the daughter of such a mother.
                  Miss Smith skipped toward her impassive papa, and seizing his chilly hand, made
                     a point, of carrying it to her lips, and said—
                  "Permit me, dearest father, to have the pleasure of presenting you to a
                     delightful new friend, for whose acquaintance we are indebted to cousin Jack.
                     Mr. Grandval, my father will appreciate you, I assure you."
                  With great show of courtesy for him, Mr. Smith put forth his hand, and took
                     that of our hero in its clammy clasp, while he scanned him with quite an
                     appreciative glance, for he was well aware of the game for a husband which was
                     then in progress.
                  "Glad to see you sir: hope we shall often have that pleasure in future," said
                     Mr. Smith with great appearance of cordiality for one of his reserved demeanor;
                     but to the Southerner his manner seemed barely civil.
                  After some conversation, which was an odd jumble of fashion on the part of Mrs.
                     Smith, sentiment on that of her daughter, sporting on that of cousin Jack, and
                     commercial affairs on the part of the merchant and his son, a waiter was
                     brought in containing fruits and wine.
                     Miss Smith did the honors so gracefully, and had so many witty or sentimental
                     toasts to propose, that when the hour of departure at length arrived, our hero
                     was in quite a state of exhilaration. He squeezed Miss Smith's hand at parting,
                     and breathed a few words in the vicinity of her long curls, which caused her to
                     blush and smile sweetly as she said—
                  "She would think of it."
                  With the assistance of Medler, Grandval was safely seated in a cab; for, to
                     tell the truth, he was not in the habit of drinking, and the wine he had taken
                     produced such a confusion in his brain, that he was not very capable of judging
                     which would be the best method of getting into the vehicle; but seemed to have
                     a very decided opinion that to climb over the back part, would be the most
                     eligible.
                  Medler at last succeeded in depositing him in his own room in safety, and left
