                     him to his slumbers, with the comfortable conviction in his own mind, that his
                     young friend had decidedly committed himself to his fair cousin; and unless he
                     had more nerve than he gave him credit for possession, Miss Kate Smith would
                     soon be Mrs. Grandval.
                  On the following morning, our hero awoke with a violent head-ache, and a
                     feeling of decided disgust toward the reminiscenses of the past evening. It was
                     late in the day, and he hastily rang his bell; a servant soon answered the
                     summons, and he ordered soda water and a shower bath. When the man returned, on
                     the waiter beside the soda bottle, lay a delicate rose-colored billet, sealed
                     with Cupid's bow, and highly perfumed.
                  "Where did this come from?" he asked in surprise.
                  "Mr. Medler met me on the stairs, and told me to deliver it to you, sir."
                  With many misgivings, Grandval took the note, and motioned to the man to leave
                     him alone. With nervous trepidation he broke the seal, and in the tiniest and
                     most elegant caligraphy, he found merely an invitation from, Mrs. Smith to a
                     musical soirée at her house on the following evening. To this, Miss Smith had
                     added on her behalf a few lines, hoping they would not be disappointed in
                     seeing him, and hinting very intelligibly, that a call would in the meantime be
                     very acceptable.
                  "Your parting words," concluded the young lady, "gave rise to many waking
                     visions of a most Eden-like nature, and when I slept, the same rosy hues
                     pervaded my dreams.
                  "What the deuce did I say?" thought the bewildered youth. "I have not the
                     slightest recollection of what occurred for half an hour before I left. I
                     surely did not make love to this 'counterfeit presentment' of my adorable
                     Mary."
                  While endeavoring to recall the events of the latter portion of his visit, he
                     completed his toilette, and having ascertained that Medler was in his room,
                     proceeded thither to demand an explanation of that gentleman; for as Miss Smith
                     signed herself Kate and not Mary, his perfidious confidant must have been fully
                     aware that she was not the lady he sought.
                  He found Medler seated in a chair which rested on two legs, with his feet
                     mounted on the back of another, while he enjoyed the delights of a cigar.
                  "Come in, old fellow—glad to see you all right. Pretty well used up last night,
                     eh? What with the wine, and the bright eyes of my handsome cousin, you were
                     quite bewildered, or enthused, to use a convenient word of modern carriage. Sit
                     down, and make yourself comfortable. Had a note from Kate this morning, I know.
                     What did the sweet sparkler have to say?"
                  Grandval sat down, looking very grave.
                  "So her name is Kate, and yet you took me to visit her, when I expressly stated
                     that my Miss Smith is named Mary. Why have you acted thus, Mr.
                     Medler? I confided in you in good faith, and certainly expected to be treated
                     differently."
                  "My dear boy, do not take this affair so seriously, or I shall never pardon
                     myself. These Smiths are charming people and very agreeable acquaintances. To
                     tell you the simple truth, I thought, as Mary Smith is only a passing steamboat
                     acquaintance, you might find her rivaled by her interesting namesake, although
                     she had the misfortune to have been baptized Kate instead of Mary. She is a
                     lovely girl—nay, a splendid one, in my opinion, and in that of many others too,
                     for Kate Smith is quite a toast in a certain set, which is well entitled to
                     rank among the upper ten."
                  "All that may be very true, Mr. Medler, but she is not the girl I am in search
                     of, and you knew she was not, when you took me to see her. I am aware that I
                     made a fool of myself last night, after drinking the wine which your fair
                     friend almost forced on me, and this note is the result. Read it—as you are
                     doubtless familiar with her tactics it can be no violation of confidence."
                  "There you're mistaken," replied Medler coolly. "It would take a wiser man than
                     Napoleon and Wellington together, to understand the tactics of any woman; but I
                     have no objection to read Kate's note. She is a devilish smart girl, and always
                     writes to the purpose. Hum—invitation to a party. Of course you'll accept; for
                     it is something to again the entree to such a fashionable house as that of my
                     cousin's the Jeremiah Smiths. Eden-like, rose-hued—ah—h—girlish
                     nonsense—nothing more. I dare say Kate has said as much to twenty others, and
                     nothing came of it. You see she is still Miss Smith."
                  "And Miss Smith she is likely to remain so far as I am concerned," replied
                     Grandval deceidedly, "therefore I considered it best to decline this
                     invitation."
                  "Afraid to trust yourself in such fascinating society, eh? Well, if
                        that is your motive, I cannot blame you. Kate is a charming
                     girl, and not used to be slighted; but I dare say I can make an apology that
                     will be received But really, now, Grandval, I think you are too scrupulous.
                     Take the goods the gods provide, and be thankful. I thought I was doing quite a
                     good thing for you, when I introduced you into a family of consideration like
                     that of the Jeremiah Smiths. You know a stranger may be rich, well bred, and
                     all that, and yet fail to get in a good set in a city like New York, for want
                     of proper introductions."
                  "That is very true," replied our hero, a little shaken in his determination,
                     for he was fond of society, and as he intended remaining some time in the city,
                     he was naturally desirous of forming eligible acquaintances.
                  Medler saw his advantage, and hastened to follow it up with so many arguments
                     in favor of accepting the invitation that at last Grandval yielded. The next
                     concession to be obtained was a morning call, which Medler had predetermined
                     should be a tète a tète between his fair
                     cousin and his young friend; but this Grandval anticipated, and insisted so
                     decidedly on his remaining in the room during the few moments devoted to a
                     fashionable visit, that he was compelled to yield.
                  These prelimnaries arranged, they entered Medler's cab and soon arrived at the
                     door of Mr. Jeremiah Smith. The parlors were judiciously darkened, and Miss
                     Kate overdressed but looking very handsome, received them with great empressement. Fearing her manner might be too
                     demonstrative, and our young gentleman take the alarm, Medler found means to
                     whisper,
                  "Be cautious, Kate—the bird is shy."
                  Thus warned, Miss Smith played her part admirably; sprightly, graceful, and
                     amusing she detained them more than half an hour beyond the orthodox limits of
                     a fashionable morning call, and on their return Grandval voluntarily
                     acknowledged to Medler, that she was "very fascinating."
                  This was duly reported to Miss Smith, and with great elation of spirits she
                     prepared for a coup de main at the
                     soiree.
                  In due time Mr. Medler presented himself at the mansion of Mr. Jeremiah Smith
                     on the appointed evening, accompanied by his young friend. On entering the
                     hall, they were greeted with notes of preparation in the shape of long drawn
                     sounds from a bass viol, which seemed like prolonged snores suddenly broken
                     off. This was accompanied by the squeaking of wind-instruments, and no wonder
                     they gave forth distorted sounds, for human ingenuity seemed to have tortured
                     itself to produce such hideous inventions as many of them were.
                  These instruments were presided over by dark snuffy-looking gentleman, with a
                     superfluity of moustache and whiskers, and very luxurious crops of hair.
                  
                  Miss Smith, dressed as a soi disant
                     representative of St. Cecilia, stood beside a harp which was ornamented with
                     wreaths of artificial flowers, she had a very vague idea of the costume
                     properly belonging to the patroness of music, for in the pictures of that
                     saintly lady which she had seen, there was a scantiness of attire which might
                     be appropriate to a saint, but would certainly have argued a deficiency in
                     laces and muslins on the part of her earthly representative, had she closely
                     imitated her model.
                  Miss Smith considered her costume a happy and medium between modern fashion and
                     classic taste. Over a white silk under-dress, she wore several skirts of
                     gossamer material, embroidered at the edges in an oak-leaf pattern which had
                     quite a rich effect. Her arms were bare to the shoulder, and glittering with
                     bracelets; her dress was cut very low on the bosom, and a species of Roman toga
                     made of lace and embroidered to match her dress, was thrown over her fair neck,
                     and fastened over the left shoulder with an immense cameo. Her hair was drawn
                     back and twisted in a classic knot at the back of the head, from which a few
                     curls escaped, and her brow was encircled with a wreath of laurel.
                  Grandval was quite struck with her appearance and agreed with Mr. Medler's
                     asertion, that she was a magnificent looking girl, and would reflect honor upon
                     the man who should be so fortunate as to win her consent to take him for better
                     or for worse.
                  It had occurred to Mrs. Smith, that as her daughter was dressed in character,
                     it would be quite as appropriate for the mistress of the mansion to be so too,
                     and she accordingly decided to appear as Minerva; the only token thereof being
                     a sort of helmet made of-black velvet and surmounted by a plume of marabout
                     feathers; the last being an innovation of her own, and considered by her as
                     quite an improvement. The remainder of her dress was in the height of the last
                     fashion plate, consequently Minerva would have been at a loss to recognize her
                     representative, had she looked in on the assemblage.
                  On this occasion Miss Angelica Smith, the second daughter, who was quite a
                     musical prodigy, was permitted to appear in society. She was a young lady of
                     tall and rather attenuated proportions, who looked as if she had outgrown her
                     clothes; for her dress was absurdly short, and was eked out by continuations of
                     pink satin, made a la Turque, and buttoned
                     above a white satin slipper, certainly not of fairy-like proportions. She was
                     sallow dull-looking, and seemed as if she felt herself out of place in a grown
                     up assemblage, for although pretty well grown herself, she had been carefully
                     kept in the back ground, and made to dress as a child until her elder sister
                     should obtain an establishment.
                  Mr. Smith, looking as white and cold as ever, played the part of the courteous
                     host and Mr. Jonas, as brown and dry as on their first meeting, accosted
                     Grandval and seizing him by the button commenced a dissertation on the state of
                     the money market, which was only ended by the united crash of all the
                     instruments performing an overture.
                  Young ladies in white, blue, and pink, adorned with streaming ribbons of all
                     the colors of the rainbow, and the gayest flowers and jewels, fluttered through
                     the rooms attended by cavaliers attired in the last mode.
                     Altogether it was quite a fashionable and distingushed gathering, and Miss
                     Smith thought her new acquaintance must be favorably impressed with the
                     position her family held in society.
                  
                  Our hero we are sorry to say, had not much taste for the kind of music he was
                     now stunned with. He liked a ballad, sung with sweetness and simplicity by some
                     loved voice, and many of the negro melodies he had heard chanted by the slaves
                     when at work in his native fields possessed a great charm for him; but
                     scientific music he could not appreciate. He however applauded with the rest,
                     and enthusiastically encored Miss Smith when she performed a solo on her
                     flower-laden harp. This was followed by a very loud and very shrill performance
                     by Miss Angelica, which the bearded gentlemen of the musical profession,
                     pronounced "magnificent."
                  Grandval had no doubt it was so, but he had more than once been tempted during
                     its continuance to seek relief for his tortured ears by a retreat to the
                     balcony; and would doubtless have done so had not the contortions of the
                     performer held him spell-bound. Apprehensive that they might end in spasms, he
                     looked around for some restorative in case of need, and seeing nothing better
                     than a pitcher of lemonade which stood on a side table, he mentally resolved on
                     a shower bath of that should it prove necessary.
                  Fortunately the performance ended without any fatal catastrophe, and then there
                     was a recess to allow refreshments of the lightest description to be handed
                     among the crowd, for Mrs. Smith fully understood the impolicy of over eating
                     for those who were to exercise their vocal powers, and her musical soirees,
                     among her own set, had long since obtained the soubriquet of starvation
                     parties.
                  During the evening Miss Smith had so successfully maneuvred as to keep Grandval
                     near herself, and he was rather amused to observe the evident annoyance this
                     occasioned to a small pink-looking man, with particularly light hair and eyes,
                     who colored if any one looked at him. He kept a strict watch over all the
                     movements of Miss Smith, and seemed amazed and confounded that she should over
                     look himself in favor of any other person.
                  At the conclusion of the performance, when she arose from her seat this
                     gentleman advanced, with a timid and awkward air of
                     hesitation, as if to offer his arm; she looked over him in the coolest manner
                     and, with a smile and nod, invited our hero to join in the promenade which the
                     rest of the company had already commenced.
                  "I know you have enjoyed the music," she said, "for you have a soul attuned to
                     harmony, I am sure your looks indicate it."
                  Grandval bowed, glad she took it for granted that it had afforded him pleasure,
                     for he could not conscientiously say so himself, and he was too polite to be
                     willing to confess the truth.
                  "Here is a waiter of refreshments, Mr. Grandval. Will you take something?"
                  "Nothing, thank you—shall I help you?"
                  "O, no—at musical parties it seems to me that we should forget our mundane
                     nature, and only exist on the nectar of sweet sounds. Let us make our way to
                     the balcony. Do you know that has become a hallowed spot to me since those
                     happy moments I enjoyed there in your society?"
                  Grandval was naturally a bashful man, though no one could accuse him of
                     timidity. He blushed at this avowal, and an observer might have mistaken his
                     embarrasment for that of a doubtful lover, in place of suspecting the actual
                     predicament in which he found himself, of being made love to by a very stylish
                     and elegant looking girl.
                  Uncertain how to act, he suffered himself to be led like a lamb to sacrifice,
                     and in a few moments found himself alone with Miss Smith on the balcony, for
                     the remainder of the company were not so ethereal as she professed to be, and
                     they were in the enjoyment of such creature comforts as had been provided for
                     them in the shape of float, which consisted of an island of pink froth resting
                     on a sea of skimmed milk, seasoned with sugar and wine. This was accompanied by
                     the more solid edibles of sponge cake and candies.
                  Miss Smith drew her reluctant companion to the place on which they had sat a
                     few evenings before, and turning her melting eyes upon him, with their most be
                     witching expression said,
                  "Do you remember the simple ballad with which I closed our charmed interview on
                     the evening of our first happy meeting? O halcyon hour, long to be cherished as
                     the brightest souvenir of my existence!"
                  "I—I—am sure you will pardon me, Miss Smith, but—I—I—really, I have been so
                     dull as to forget."
                  "Forget!" she exclaimed with a tragic start, while her eyes changed their
                     languishing for a most upbraiding expression—"Forget! thus it ever is with your
                     most perfidious sex. You practice your deluding flatteries but to decieve."
                  "Upon my word you misunderstood me," said the wretched Grandval, "I think you a
                     lady of transcendent charms, and, if my heart were free to choose, I should
                     doubtless have been deeply impressed by them; but, really, I must speak the
                     truth—"
                  
                  "What is it?" she faintly asked, "Is it not true that you love me, as your
                     words led me to believe on the evening of our first interview?"
                  'I—I had no intention to deceive you Miss Smith. I am sorry that you should
                     have misinterpreted mere words of unmeaning gallantry. Your cousin, Mr. Medler,
                     could have told you that I am desperately in love with
                     another lady."
                  "Unmeaning gallantry—love another," murmured the fair Kate.
                        "O, cruel cruel, my heart is broken by the
                        unmanly trifling. What did you whisper in my ear on your
                     departure on that first evening? Words which I have dwelt on so fondly—clasped
                     to my heart with such passionate joy—and they were mere professions; I grow
                     faint, I believe I am dying."
                  There was no resource left for the unhappy young man, and he was compelled to
                     offer his arm as a support to the tottering frame of the young lady. Her head
                     rested on his shoulder, and her lips almost came in contact with his cheek,
                     even in this bewildering position, his curiority, to ascertain what he really
                     had said to her, triumphed over every other feeling and he urgently asked,
                  "What could I have said to produce such on unhappy crisis as the present
                     one?"
                  "Said, ah cruel monster, you feign forgetfulness," gasped the young lady,
                  "Upon my soul I do not. I am serious enough, now I assure you."
                  "And you were not serious when you clasped my hand, and asked me if I would
                     sing, 'Am I not fondly thine own?' when we next met?"
                  "Ah h," sighed Grandval, infinately relieved, "Was that all?"
                  "All," murmured she faintly "was not that enough?"
                  At that moment she caught a glimpse of a figure standing in the French window,
                     and became extremely ill on the instant. Her form fell
                     heavier on his arm, and he was about to call for assistance,
                     reckless of what might ensue, when Mr. Jonas came forward and said,
                  "What on earth is the matter? Has Kate lost her senses, that she acts such a
                     scene where strangers may see her?"
                  "I believe she has," said Grandval, hastily transferring the drooping form to
                     young Smith, "If she has not, I must have lost mine. I will send Mrs. Smith, or
                     the other young lady."
                  Thus speaking he hurriedly passed into the house, but as he had a shrewd
                     suspicion that Miss Kate's indisposition was only a feint he did not trouble
                     himself to find the representative of Minerva, but seized the first hat he came
                     to, and rushing into the open air walked rapidly toward his boarding-house in a
                     perfect fever of anger and indignation.
                  He saw through it all, and Medler's anxiety to take him to this party was
                     explained. A regular net had been spread for him in the belief that he would be
                     dull enough to be snared in its meshes. They had deceived themselves, and that
                     was some comfort, though the reflection did not abate his resentment at being
                     supposed a fit victim for so shallow a plot.
                  On the following morning before Grandval had arisen, two missives were brought
                     to him. One was from Mr. Jonas Smith, demanding the cause of his singular
                     escapade on the preceding evening; and very intelligibly hinting at the
                     comfortable alternative of matrimony with his fair and broken-hearted sister,
                     or pistols for two. Grandval had expected something of the kind, and had
                     already made up his mind as to the course he should pursue.
                  On opening the other letter, he found the following words:
                  
                     
                        
                           
                              "SIR:—Although I am not a fighting man, and am incapacitated from
                                 it by shortness of sight, and a degree of nervousness which is very
                                 painful, still I cannot permit my claims to be trampled on, and my
                                 happiness destroyed, without an effort on my part to avert such
                                 disastrous consequences, though I have never had courage to speak
                                 to Miss Smith on the subject, still a perfect understanding has
                                 existed between us for some time, that when I could
                                 manage to put my devotion in words, she would not reject it. I am a
                                 painfully bashful man, and if you have any sympathy you must feel
                                 it for one situated as I am. This his young lady is probably the
                                 only one I shall ever have courage to approach as a lover, while
                                 such a dashing blade as yourself may select among the fairest.
                              Pray take these circumstances into consideration Mr. Grandval, and
                                 withdraw from your pursuit of Miss Smith while there is yet time;
                                 for in case of a refusal, I must resort to the barbarous and
                                 uncivilized custom of the duels, which is so strangely sustained
                                 among a people who profess to be enlightened and christianized.
                              I shall the more willingly do this, as life will
                                 be shorn of all its promised joy, if you bear off from me the
                                 affections of the girl I prefer above all others. With respect,
                              
                                 MICAJAH SIMMONS.
                              
                           
                           
                              P. S. Do not consider this last clause in the light of a threat,
                                 for I am emphatically a man of peace, and would very much prefer an
                                 amicable settlement of this difficulty; which settlement must, of
                                 course, involve the quiet resignation of the lady in dispute, to
                                 him who has prior claims upon her affections.
                              
                                 M. S."
                              
                           
                        
                     
                  
                  This singular epistle Grandval read several times, and in spite of the
                     vexatious predicament in which he found himself, he laughed long and loud over
                     it. Mr. Micajah Simmons was evidently a character, and but for the absurd
                     rivalry in which they were placed, he would certainly have sought his
                     acquaintance. He had little difficulty in identifying him with the pink-looking
                     gentleman with light hair, who had been treated so cavalierly by Miss Smith on
                     the previous evening. He seized a pen, and immediately replied—
                  
                     
                        
                           
                              "MR SIMMONS:—God forbid that I should take either your life or your
                                 love. Retain them both, I beg, and long may you continue to do so,
                                 is the earnest wish of him you have honored with your eloquent
                                 appeal.
                              Miss Smith is nothing more to me than the passing acquaintance of
                                 the hour, though I consider her a very charming girl, and am not
                                 surprised at your devotion to her. Since my
                                    visits have interfered so materially with
                                 your happiness, I shall cease to make them in future, and you may
                                 consider the field as indisputably your own.
                              Permit me, in conclusion, to give you a piece of advice. Overcome
                                 your bashfulness and give language to your passion, or some youth
                                 more enamored than myself, may step in and bear off the young lady
                                 from one whose devotion renders him so eminently worthy of her.
                              
                                 Yours very respectfully,
                                 M. GRANDVAL."
                              
                           
                        
                     
                  
                  The reception of Mr. Simmons' communication a little
                     modified his course toward Mr Jonas Smith. He enclosed that gentleman's letter
                     to him with the following words—
                  
                     
                        
                           
                              "From the enclosed you may see that your fair sister will not be
                                 very materially damaged in her affections by our brief
                                 acquaintance. If, as I have every right to believe, Miss Smith
                                 wishes to obtain an establishment of her own, the devotion of Mr.
                                 Simmons places it in her power to do so; whereas a duel, and all
                                 the idle gossip which it must give rise to, will very seriously
                                 compromise her chance of such a denouement.
                              As to myself, I assure you, I am neither to be wheedled nor bullied
                                 into matrimony. My youth may have led you into the error of
                                 supposing me entirely inexperienced in affairs of this kind; but
                                 such is not the case. I have already been out once, and although it
                                 would be repugnant to my feelings to do so in the present instance,
                                 on account of the ridiculous misconception out of which this whole
                                 affair has arisen; still I will fight sooner than become an object
                                 of contempt to myself, as that most egregious of all fools, a man
                                 thrust into the fetters of Hymen without his own full and free
                                 consent.
                              Yours, with or without pistols, as you may decide,
                              
                                 M. GRANDVAL."
                              
                           
                        
                     
                  
                  These letters despatched, Grandval inquired for Medler, but that gentleman had
                     wisely remembered a promise to visit some friends in the country, and had
                     departed at an early hour, leaving this untoward affair to be settled before
                     his return.
                  Grandval received no further communication from Mr. Jonas Smith, and he
                     concluded the young gentleman had decided in the present instance, that
                     "discretion was the better part of valor." We may as well mention here that,
                     before the summer was over, or his own quest completed, he accidentally saw the
                     marriage of Mr. Micajah Simmons to Miss Katharine Smith announced in one of the city papers; thus proving that Mr.
                     Simmons had profited by his advice.
                  Our hero, now left to his own resources, took to wandering about the streets,
                     and reading the names on the plates, in the hope that he might find a John
                     Smith whose residence looked as if it might shelter the object of his pursuit.
                     He found this so tiresome a proceeding that he finally procured a city
                     directory, and turned to the column dedicated to the Smiths. This capped the
                     climax of his bewilderment, for among them were no less than thirty John
                     Smiths, scattered widely apart, and inhabiting sections of the city as diverse
                     as were their fortunes.
                  Still the quest must be followed; for, in addition to the love he cherished for
                     the fair Mary, his pride was now enlisted in bringing the pursuit to a
                     successful termination. Besides, he felt in his heart a deep conviction that
                     this young girl must certainly become his wife, and no other. With this belief,
                     he was only inspired with new energy by every new defeat.
                  "I must find her," was the first thought of his waking hours, and
                     the last that remained with him before he lost all consciousness in his nightly
                     slumbers Mrs. Wilson regarded his infatuation as a species of monomania, and
                        vainly endeavered to dissuade him from any further
                     attempt to discover his lost love.
                  Grandval now concluded to employ a cab to drive slowly through the streets of
                     the city, and pause near the location of some one of the John Smiths, while he
                     alighted and reconoitered the premises, with a strong belief that when he came
                     to the right one some intuitive conviction would flash upon his mind that he
                     had at last reached the desired haven.
                  Thus occupied many days and each evening he returned in a state of despondency;
                     for although he found the homes of the Smiths, from the stately mansion of
                     wealth to the humble cottage of the poor, he failed to discover one he dared
                     approach with the belief that his Mary dwelled therein as a holy presence.
                  The cabman thought him deranged, but he was well paid for
                     attending to so harmless a whim, and he looked as particularly after our hero
                     as if he had been some lost child he was employed to restore to his afflicted
                     parents. He continued this quest more than a week, and was really beginning to
                     despond; when, one evening, as he was proceeding slowly through one of the
                     thoroughfares leading from the city, an open carriage drawn by two magnificent
                     bays came dashing up the street. It whirled past like a flash, but in that
                     brief moment a young, fair face was turned toward Grandval; a look of surprised
                     recognition and a salutation came to him at the same moment; starting up, he
                     exclaimed:
                  "'Tis she, 'tis herself, at last! Turn, quick—after that carriage, driver, and
                     if you overtake it I will double, treble your fare!'
                  Though the man did not comprehend the cause of this excitement, he understood
                     the offer of increased pay very well, and as quickly as possible obeyed his
                     command: but, alas! their horse was already wearied, and before they succeeded
                     in obtaining another view of the carriage, which had passed at so rapid a pace,
                     it was already half-a-mile ahead of them. Grandval seized the whip, and in a
                     state of mind bordering on distraction, belabored the unlucky animal until he
                     exasperated his wearied limbs into a gallop; but a few minutes convinced him
                     that the chase was desperate, and he sank back in a state of discouragement,
                     from which the recollection of the passing smile that beamed upon him, could
                     alone arouse him.
                  As they failed to overtake them, it then occurred to Grandval that the
                     occupants of the carriage lived in the environs of the city, in the direction
                     whence it came, and if he remained long enough, the vehicle would return. He
                     ordered the driver to draw up to one side of the road, while he watched for the
                     re-appearance of the carriage, with the desperate resolution to stop the
                     driver, speak to Miss Smith, and request the favor of her address, with a view
                     of calling on her. This proceeding was, he knew, quite out of rule, but his
                     case was nearly desperate, and he must resort to a desperate remedy, trusting
                     to his eloquence to obtain forgiveness, when he could once
                     more unrestrainedly converse with his idol.
                  Many vehicles of every description passed, and once our lover was deluded into
                     the belief that he was about to succeed in stopping the right one, when the
                     sound of a deep curse from a gruff voice, arrested his hand in the act of
                     making a signal to the driver. Daylight waned, and the gas
                     was lighted, yet Grandval still clung to the fallacious hope that she would
                     return. Nine, ten, eleven pealed forth from the clock on a neighboring steeple,
                     and still he seemed as much on the qui vive
                     as ever.
                  At length the patience of the good-natured driver was exhausted, and he
                     said:—
                  "Look'e here, Mr. Granwil, you are a very nice sort o' gentleman, only a leetle
                     touched in the upper story, and I am willin' to let you have your own way wi'
                     my cab consarn, as long as it's reasonable, 'specially as you pays well; but
                     this passes mortal patience. It is now 'leven o'clock, and my hoss is in a
                     starvin' condition; he have traveled pretty near all day, a huntin' up these
                     Smiths what never turn out to be the right ones, and I think it's time for all
                     rational people to go home, and quietly forget their whimsies in a comfortable
                     sleep."
                  Grandval sighed deeply.
                  "You are right, Brian. There is now no hope that she will return. Drive to Mrs.
                     Wilson's, and be sure to come back to-morrow at an early hour. I have a new
                     idea which may lead to success at last."
                  "She will return," thought Brian, as he put his horse in motion
                     once more. "Ah, I might a' known that a woman is at the bottom of all this
                     scrimble scramble after the Smiths. Lord help the lad! there can't be much
                     difficulty in finding plenty of gals of that name, though may be
                     in the muss he can't get at the right one."
                  Our hero did not sleep much that night, but when slumber did descend upon his
                     eyelids, he had most enchanting dreams of his charming Mary, and consequently
                     arose the following morning in excellent spirits. Brian was punctual in obeying
                     his orders, and he commanded him to take the same road they had traveled on the
                     preceding evening. They continued to drive on until the open country, thinly
                     dotted over with residences, was attained. Then Grandval checked their speed,
                     and critically examined the different places as they slowly passed them. They
                     at length came to a summer residence built in the Moorish style, and the yard
                     was embellished with statues and vases filled with rare flowers. A closely
                     shaven lawn was shaded by a groupe of fine trees, beneath which gamboled a pet
                     deer, and a pair of goats, decorated with blue ribbons.
                  "This, surely, must be the abode of my Peri," thought the enraptured youth;
                     "and those beautiful animals are her playful companions. She betrays the
                     tenderness of her heart even in her love for the brute creation."
                  He alighted, entered the enclosure, and loitered toward the house, stopping by
                     the way to form an acquaintance with the deer, which bounded toward him with
                     the familiarity of a favored pet, as soon as he descried him. Grandval had not
                     quite made up his mind as to the course he should pursue, and he was glad to
                     gain a few moments for reflection, while he caressingly smoothed the glossy
                     coat of his new friend.
                  With a quickly palpitating heart he at length approached the light verandah,
                     entered, and rang at the door. A respectable servant appeared, and, he thought,
                     eyed him somewhat suspiciously; he however replied respectfully to the inquiry
                     our adventurer had resolved to hazard, as to whether Mr. Smith was at home.
                  "No, sir," replied the man. "Mr. Smith has gone to the city on particular
                     business, and will not be back before night."
                  This was a disappointment; but something was gained in ascertaining that this
                     was really the domicile of Mr. Smith. Grandval took courage to ask—
                  "Can I have the pleasure of seeing the young lady of the house in the absence
                     of her father?"
                  "The young ladies have also gone to the city, sir, to spend several days"
                  "Ah," thought Grandval, "they were on their way when I met them last
                     evening."
                  He sighed, and was turning away, when the man said—
                  
                  "Any name, sir? The young ladies is very pertickler about the gentlemen as
                     calls in their absence."
                  Grandval drew forth a card, and handed it to him. He proceeded slowly down the
                     walk, so pleasantly shaded, and inhaled the fragrant breath
                     of the flowers, with the intoxicating belief that the presence of his Mary had
                     recently hallowed the very ground on which he trod.
                  He could not resolve to return to town, but dismissed Brian with directions to
                     come for him to the same place in the evening. Then striking into the woods, he
                     spent the day in the rural companionship of the birds and insects which inhabit
                     the sports that man has not invaded. The Moorish castle was viewed from every
                     possible point of view, and sketches of it transferred to his pocket book, to
                     be retained as souvenirs of the auspicious day which witnessed the discovery of
                     the abode of his beloved.
                  Late in the evening, Brian in some uneasiness of mind, returned to the place
                     agreed on for he had many misgivings as to Grandval's capacity to take care of
                     himself even for a single day, but he was much relieved to find him walking
                     slowly toward the place of rendezvous, in quite a sane condition.
                  The following day was spent in a similar manner, for there was an attraction
                     about the supposed residence of the fair Mary, that was irresistible, although
                     he knew it did not then shelter her. Late in the evening a close carriage was
                     driven to the door, and two ladies alighted from it. Grandval was not near
                     enough to distinguish features, but he saw that the taller of the two, was
                     about the size of her he was in pursuit of, and he gazed on her form as long as
                     it was visible, with a strong inclination to rush forward, and claim her
                     acquaintance. He however remembered that the day had been warm and dusty—and he
                     had lounged about under trees in grassy places until his clothes would be much
                     more becoming to a gentleman of his condition, if they had a through brushing.
                     Taking this into consideration, he refrained from making his presence known,
                     and walked slowly forward to meet his cab.
                  The next day he arose in great elation of spirits, and set forth upon his
                     pilgrimage to the shrine of his divinity. At an hour suitable for such calls,
                     he was set down at the gate of the Moorisa Castle. Wishing to enjoy the
                     promenade to the house, he would not permit Brian to enter the enclosure, but
                     commanded him to draw up under the shadow of some trees on the roadside. The
                     bright sunshine was playing on the grassy lawn; the pets were frolicking in its
                     beams, and accompanying them was a slender girl clad in white, with a green
                     vail thrown carelessly over her head.
                  Certain that this must be the Miss Smith, Grandval was seized with
                     such a fluttering at the heart, that he was forced to pause some moments before
                     he could summon courage to advance upon the gravelled walk. She was stooping
                     over, and so busily engaged arranging the ribbons on the collar of her deer,
                     that she was not aware of his presence, until he came quite near, and
                     spoke.
                  "Pardon me Miss Smith, but I have presumed on the acquaintance formed—"
                  He paused horror-struck, for at the sound of his voice, the young lady raised
                     her head, turned quickly, and throwing aside her vail, revealed the face of an
                     utter stranger! A face too as unlike that of the true Mary, as it was possible
                     for human features to be. Brown as a berry, with a snub-nose, very full lips,
                     and light grey eyes, she looked to Grandval absolutely hideous. She surveyed
                     him with surprize, and then said—
                  "Excuse me sir, but this seems to me a very singular intrusion, especially as
                     my name is not Smith but Jones."
                  Grandval could have torn his hair with vexation.—"I—I—really, Miss Jones, I
                     have but one explanation to offer, which is that I have played the fool most
                     egregiously. Pardon me for interupting you, and I will make the only atonement
                     in my power, by retiring as speedily as possible. Good morning."
                  He was turning away overwhelmed with confusion, when a loud voice shouted—
                  "Hillo! young man, not so fast if you please. A word with you, before you
                     go."
                  Our hero looked in the direction of the voice, and saw a fat, red faced man,
                     attended by an enormous dog, advancing rapidly upon him.
                  "Mr. Jones I presume," said Grandval, with a polite bow, as he came within
                     speaking distance, "I am very sorry, sir, that a misconception on my part,
                     should have led to so unpleasant a——"
                  "Fal-lal—words—words—I know," interrupted the choloric gentleman.
                     "Are you not the same fellow that called here two days ago, and inquired for
                     Mr. Smith?"
                  Before Grandval could recover from his astonishment at this address, made in so
                     discourteous a manner, a voice at his elbow replied for him,
                  "Yes, sir, it is the same, I'll take my affidavy of it."
                  Looking around, the young man saw that the servant who had answered his
                     inquiries on the occasion of his first call at the Castle had advanced on them
                     by a side path.
                  "I'm not disposed to deny it," replied Grandval with as much dignity as he
                     could assume, "I was under the impression that a gentleman of that name resided
                     here, and I called to renew an acquaintance with himself and his daughter
                     which—"
                  "Well, sir," interrupted the fat man, "a gentleman of that name does reside
                     here, and that is myself. I am Smith—Green Smith at your service
                     sir; now what have you to say ssr, for you are no acquaintance of
                     mine.'
                  "Nothing, sir, except that you are not the person I expected to find; and I
                     will add that just now, Red Smith would be a more suitable name for you."
                  "While you appropriate my soubriquet, eh? said Mr. Smith, "I must
                     acknowledge it would fit you well; any one could have told you that this is the
                     residence of John Jones, Esq., who I flatter myself is sufficiently well known
                     to the world in general, as one of the millionaires of our land; and I, sir, am
                     his brother-in-law. Your intrusion, sir, is so unwarrantable, and made under so
                     flimsy a pretext, that after the occurrences of last night, I feel it my duty
                     to arrest you."
                  "Arrest me, and by what authority? For what offense? To what occurrences do you
                     allude, that can give you a shadow of excuse for such an indignity?" asked our
                     hero angrily.
                  "O, of course you are very indignant, but the circumstances are suspicious
                     enough to bear me out in the course I intend to pursue. You called here two
                     days ago, hoping by your genteel appearance to gain admittance as a visitor,
                     when you could make such observations as you desired for the furtherance of
                     your purpose. Baffled in that by the absence of the family you pretended to
                     know, you lurk around the premises for two days, spying out their most
                     accessible points, and last night your companions came, doubtless accompanied
                     by yourself, and robbed the house of valuables to the amount of over a thousand
                     dollars."
                  At this unlooked for charge, Grandval stood aghast. Amid all the difficulties
                     he might encounter in his search after happiness he had never anticipated the
                     possibility of such an accusation being preferred against him. He said,
                  "Really, sir, if this were not so absured as to be amusing, I could find it in
                     my heart to be angry at having such language used to me. I am a gentleman as
                     you may see, if you have any knowledge of what constitutes that manner of
                     man."
                  "O, yes," sneered Mr. Smith, "You of the swell mob are always gentlemen I know,
                     so far as dress can make you so, and your outfit is
                     undeniable, however it won't do. I shall take you to the police office in the
                     city, and prefer my charge at once, and I do not think there will be much
                     chance to disprove either."
                  "I warn you, sir, that you do this at your peril, I can easily prove myself
                     above all suspicion of such villainy as you accuse me of and I will not permit
                     myself to be thus outraged and insulted, without seeking such redress as the
                     law allows."
                  "I'll take the consequences young man, and I feel myself amply able to bear
                     them.—Thomas did you not see this person examining the house from every
                     direction for two days before the robbery was committed?"
                  "Yes, sir, his behavior was so suspicious like, that I took the spy-glass and
                     spied on him, and he made drawings of it I am certain for he used a paper and
                     pencil a many times, and he stood a long time a staring at the north window
                     through which the thieves entered."
                  
                  Grandval struck his clenched hand upon his forehead; he could have execrated
                     himself for his sentimental wanderings around the home of the hideous Miss
                     Jones, who stood an amused and interested listener to the conversation; and
                     stared at our hero through a little eye-glass suspended from her watch chain,
                     as coolly as if he had been some species of animal brought there for her
                     especial examination. He asked indignantly,
                  "And do you really mean, sir, that I shall be forced to submit to the
                     degradation of being examined before a police magistrate, on the charge of
                     robbery?"
                  "I certainly do," replied Mr. Green Smith decidedly, while he patted his dog
                     affectionately. "I would advise you not to make any attempt to escape, for this
                     animal is an ugly customer, and would spring upon you at a motion of my
                     finger."
                  "I shall make no such effort, sir. The cab that brought me hither is near the
                     gate; permit me to write a few lines to a friend of mine, to meet me at the
                     police court, and you will then see that you have been
                     betrayed into insulting an innocent man. If I had robbed your house, I should
                     scarcely have attempted to call on the family this morning."
                  "Oh, there's no telling the impudence of you gents of the flash order. Came
                     here to make love to my niece there, I shouldn't wonder."
                  At this, Grandval looked so disdainfully at the ugly little brownie, that she
                     dropped her glass, and turned away, while Mr. Green Smith went on—
                  "Well, well—write your note, and Thomas may go with the cabman, as there is no
                     knowing but he may be a confederate."
                  The young man disdained a reply, and, taking out his pocket-book, sought for a
                     piece of blank paper on which to scrawl a few lines to an old and well-known
                        merchant in New York, who was his uncle's business
                     correspondent, and through whose house he drew his own income. While thus
                     employed, a paper dropped on the grass, which was picked up by Thomas, who
                     displayed it to his master, with an exclamation.
                  "Strong presumptive evidence," said Mr. Smith, as he looked on the pencilled
                     outlines of that portion of the Moorish Castle in which was situated the
                     identical north window, through which the burglars had made their ingress.
                     "'Murder will out,' as the proverb goes; and so, for that
                     matter, will robbery, where it is quickly traced up, as I mean to follow this
                        up. What do you say to this, young man?"
                  "Nothing more than I have already said," replied Grandval, haughtily. "You will
                     soon learn that your evidence is entirely worthless, so far as I am
                     concerned."
                  He folded the few lines he had written, and politely requested the company of
                     Mr. Smith to the gate, as he wished to give his directions to the driver
                     himself. That gentleman complied, and Brian, who had discovered that something
                     strange was going on, was quite horror struck when his employer informed him of
                     the dilemma in which he was placed and desired him to permit Thomas to take his
                     seat in the cab, while he made all haste to the house of Drew & Co., and
                     delivered the note with which he was entrusted.
                  "Oh! oh!" exclaimed the poor fellow scratching his head ruefully. "I never
                     thought you was 'zackly non compos but I
                     didn't expect you would get into quite such a scrape as to
                     be tuck up for breakin' the law. Howsever, that are red-faced gentleman had
                     better mind what he is after, I can tell him."
                  "And you had better attend to what you are ordered to do, friend,"
                     replied Mr. Smith, with great sang froid, "or
                     you may find yourself placed in the same predicament as an accomplice."
                  "Oh, it's myself that is not afraid of you, nor all your kith nor kin, even if
                     you could buy the town wi' your money; for I mind my own business, and cheat no
                     man out of a penny that belongs to him. His honor, the Police Judge, knows me,
                     too, as honest Brian, for I've been afore him as a witness more than onct."
                  "Then you had better interest him for your friend here, for he will soon stand
                     in need of his good offices. Come, be off. Jump in, Thomas and be on your guard
                     that no trick is played on you. Keep a bright look out, and meet me, in an hour
                     and a half, at the police court."
                  "Oh, but he shall pay dearly for this ride in my cab," muttered Brian, as
                     Thomas stepped briskly into the vehicle, and, giving a smart cut to the horse,
                     trotted off in the direction of the city.
               
               
                  CHAPTER V.
                  CLOSELY attended by the mastiff, which showed his teeth and snailed if our hero
                     diverged a step from the pathway, the two walked back to the house, on the
                     steps of which Grandval was commanded to wait in the custody of the dog, and a
                     gardener who was summoned for the purpose.
                  Very different was this reception from the one he had anticipated as he came
                     out, and bitterly mortifying were his reflections. In a few moments a close
                     carriage with the curtains securely buttoned, was driven to the door; at the
                     request of Mr. Smith, Grandval got in, and was immediately followed by that
                     gentleman and his dog. In utter silence they rolled onward to their place of
                     destination.
                  When the carriage stopped, Mr. Smith summoned a police officer, in whose charge
                     he placed our hero, and guarded by the two, he walked into the dingy looking
                     room occupied by his honor, and his court. A party of Irish were before him,
                     for engaging in a drunken riot; and their friends, a motley crew, were grouped
                     around.
                  Grandval threw himself into a seat, and pulling his hat over his brows,
                     endeavored to conceal his features from the prying observation of the loungers
                     who always infest a scene of the kind. He earnestly hoped that Mr Drew would
                     make his appearance before the case now in progress was dismissed; for it was
                     terrible to him to have his name, and the accusation accompanying it, thus
                     brought forward. He watched the entrance with intense interest, hoping every
                     moment to see the wiry figure of his old friend appear therein, but time rolled
                     on, the rioters received their sentence and were dismissed, yet Brian nor him
                     he had been sent to summon, made not their appearance.
                  In a state of miserable wretchedness, Grandval heard the judge say to his
                     captor,
                  "Well, Mr. Smith, I hope you have obtained a clue to the affair of the robbery,
                     as you come before me thus attended. Since Mr. Jones called on me this morning
                     to lay in his complaint, some of my gang have been making efforts to trace the
                     perpetrators, but they have not yet had time to make a report; you have been
                     more successful I see."
                  "Yes, I believe I have nabbed one sure enough, he puts on the indignant, and
                     threatens me heavily if I persist in prosecuting the charge."
                  "Ah—ah, an old story. Those flash gentlemen are always innocent, according to
                     their own account. This one, however, must be a new hand, for he seems terribly
                     downhearted. Hillo, young man, look up at me, that I may see if I know your
                     face."
                  Grandval paid no attention to this command and Mr. Smith commenced a concise
                     narrative of the events of the morning, and the proofs of the prisoner's guilt,
                     which he considered conclusive.
                  Just as he was completing his statement, a slight bustle was heard without, and
                     the voice of Brian, in exulting tones, exclaimed,
                  "Hooroar, thanks to the blessed Lord, we are in time yet."
                  The next moment, a tall, thin man, with gray hair and whiskers, entered the
                     room, followed by the two messengers, who had been dispatched for him. He bowed
                     to the judge, and hurrying toward Grandval, said,
                  "God bless my soul! how could such a thing happen, as a charge of burglary to
                     be brought against you? The driver has been giving me such an account of your
                     late proceedings, as incline me to believe that he must have been
                     romancing."
                  "I have been playing the fool, I sincerely believe, Mr. Drew, but I have done
                     nothing to entitle me to such an indignity as this. Release me from durance,
                     and I will give you a true account of the cause of my visit to Mr. Jones'
                     house."
                  In the meantime Thomas had whispered a few words in Mr. Smith's ear, which
                        caused that personage to flush more
                     deeply than ever and he seemed quite discomfitted when Mr. Drew turned to the
                     judge, and said,
                  "Great injustice has been practised toward this young gentleman, Judge——I am
                     Andrew Drew, of the firm of Drew & Co., as you already know, and I assure
                     you that the prisoner is a southern gentleman of unimpeached honor, and
                     independent fortune. If this absurd charge is instantly relinquished my young
                     friend will pass over what has occurred this morning. If not, I will aid him in
                     prosecuting his false accuser with the ut most rigor of the law."
                  Both the judge and Mr. Smith were well acquainted with the character and
                     position of Mr Drew, and knew that his guarantee as to the respectability of
                     the prisoner was quite sufficient.
                  Mr. Smith hastened to apologize for the mistake he had made, and even invited
                     our hero to make another descent upon the Moorish Castle, when he should be
                     more hospitably received than on the former occasion. Grandval coldly thanked
                     him, and told him he was quite satisfied with his first experiment in that
                     quarter, and had no idea of ever making another.
                  Ashamed of the part he had played. Mr Smith silenced the audible comments of
                     Brian by a douceur sufficiently large to pay him four-fold for the seat his
                     servant had occupied in his cab, and accompanied by that observant individual,
                     he left the police office, and retired to the privacy of his own carriage, in
                     which he was driven home in a state of great chagrin at the blunder he had
                     made.
                  Grandval accepted from Mr. Drew an invitation to dine with him, and over their
                     wine he frankly related to him the origin of the difficulty
                     of the morning. He received much sage advice from the old gentleman, and
                     returned to his boarding house, resolved to profit by it.
                  The services of Brian were no longer in requisition; and the
                     whole family of Smiths appeared suddenly to have lost all interest for him. Mr.
                     Green Smith seemed to have thoroughly disgusted him with all of that name.
               
               
                  CHAPTER VI.
                  IN this reasonable state our young gentleman continued for an entire week.
                     Although his dreams were haunted by the sweet face of Mary Smith, he was firm
                     in his resolution to be wise, and no longer seek one whom, it seemed, each
                     attempt to find only plunged him into some absurd difficulty from which he was
                     extricated at the expense of his own dignity.
                  This last adventure had so thoroughly disgusted him that he
                     dreaded the idea of making another effort to discover her "whereabouts."
                  Grandval might, in time, have recovered his entire sanity, had he not
                     unfortunately strolled into the rooms of a distinguished daguerreotypist, and,
                     among the pictures hanging upon the walls, recognized the likeness of the
                     enchanting Mary. In a breathless state, he sought the proprietor, and inquired
                     of him the name of the original.
                  "It is Miss Smith. Mary, or Maria—I forget which is her name."
                  "Yes, yes; I was certain I could not be mistaken; but are you aware of the
                     residence of this young lady, or can you furnish me any clue by which I shall
                     be enabled to find her?"
                  "You seem deeply interested," said Mr. ——, with a smile. "I am sorry to say
                     that among the numerous Smiths which adorn my rooms, there are very few I could
                     undertake to find, and this young lady is not among the number. Now here is a
                     Tom Smith, a Sam Smith, and a half a dozen John Smiths. I think so large a
                     family should be as original in the selection of their names as an old
                     gentleman I once knew, who had thirteen children, to each of whom he gave an
                     appellation beginning with the letter Z. When he exhausted his vocabulary, he
                     invented names, and some of them were curious enough to identify even a
                     Smith."
                  Grandval scarcely listened to him. He had taken down the miniature, and was
                     gazing fondly upon its fair lineaments.
                  "What will induce you to sell me this picture?" he asked.
                  "Really, that is rather out of my way. It is understood, when a man of my
                     profession retains a specimen copy, that he will not part with it."
                  "It hangs upon your wall for all to gaze at; where is the difference in
                     allowing only one that liberty? I warn you," he added, with a smile, "that if
                     you refuse to part with it to me, your rooms will be feloniously entered, and
                     this one precious possession abstracted."
                  "Oh, in that case, perhaps it will be better to let you have it at a fair
                     price."
                  "Thank you. Remove it from its present setting, and put it in the handsomest
                     locket you have. Put your own valuation upon it, and I will cheerfully pay
                     it."
                  The fair image, enshrined in a golden case, was soon transferred to our
                     enraptured hero, who placed it next his heart, paid the some what exorbitant
                     demand of the artist, and returned to his own room to dream over it—to worship
                     it—to grow almost frantic with the conviction that, if the original could not
                     be discovered, the happiness of his life was forever marred.
                  At length it occurred to him that he would write to Miss Smith, detail the
                     circumstances of their first meeting, that no mistake might be made, and then
                     inform her of his fruitless quest after her charming self. No sooner was it
                     thought of, than he commenced putting it in execution. After spoiling about a
                     quire of paper, and consuming three days in the mental labor, he produced such
                     a missive as he was willing to dispatch. Then his courage failed him, and he
                     retained the letter several days, read and re-read it, made so many alterations
                     and erasures that it was necessary to recopy it. This done, in a state of
                     desperation, he hurried to the post-office and dropped it in before he had time
                     to repent, though ten minutes afterward he would have given much to be able to
                     recall it.
                  There was now no alternative but to wait for an answer with as much composure
                     as he could command. One, two, three days passed, and he was almost in a state
                     of despair, when a small white envelope, sealed with a bouquet of flowers,
                     reached him. It was redolent of patchoules; and in an ecstacy he rushed back to
                     his room, and, locking the door against all intruders, as carefully unclosed
                     the precious document as if he were afraid of hurting it by rough handling.
                  Within was a sheet of highly embossed note paper, on which fair and flowing
                     characters, to the following effect, were traced:
                  
                     
                        
                           
                              "MR. GRANDVAL: Your letter reached me in safety on the day after it
                                 was mailed, and, on reading it, I perfectly remembered our meeting
                                 on the boat, though pardon me when I state that your name had
                                 escaped my recollection. I have a singularly defective memory for
                                 names, though faces are never forgotten by me, and yours is not
                                 likely soon to pass from the tablet of one's fancy.
                              "I would comply with your request, and give you my father's address
                                 now, but as I am on the eve of visiting the country, I defer that
                                 gratification until my return, which will take place in two weeks;
                                 and, in the meantime, we can correspond. Letters are a charming
                                 medium of communication between kindred souls, and from their
                                 interchange we gain a deeper insight into the character of the
                                 writer's mind. The heart is more completely unmasked in the freedom
                                 of correspondence, and many things said by the pen that the lips
                                 would never utter.
                              "Then let us write daily, that when we meet we may be better known
                                 to each other than if hours had been spent together amid the social
                                 circle.
                              "Address your letters to the care of S. M., and they will be safely
                                 forwarded to me. Hoping to receive another of your beautiful
                                 effusions on to morrow, I remain
                              
                                 "Yours, truly,
                                 "MARY SMITH."
                              
                           
                        
                     
                  
                  Grandval read and re-read this letter in a transport of extravagant delight,
                     and he immediately set about answering it. He commenced with "Divine Mary,"
                     changed it to "Angelic Mary," and finally concluded that "Adorable Girl" would
                     be the most appropriate address. He went on with such a passionate effusion of
                     love joy and hope, that Miss Smith must have been more than mortal if she had
                     not been flattered and charmed by his eloquence.
                  
                  Her reply duly came, and every day for two weeks these mutual professionsof
                     admiration were exchanged, until he was more desperately in love than ever, and
                     considered his fair correspondent as positively pledged to become his bride. He
                     discovered the most remarkable similarity in their tastes during this romantic
                     exchange of vows. Miss Smith was evidently literary, and so was he; she
                     believed in sympathy, and involuntary attraction, and these were two of his own
                     strong points of faith; more than all, in one of her later letters she had
                     ventured to assert that she believed Grandval and herself had been created for
                     each other, and no other person could have rendered the other happy in the
                     sacred tie of marriage.
                  His elation at this exceeded all bounds, and he replied to it by such a
                     passionate outpouring of love and ecstacy that Miss Smith thought his feelings
                     were wrought up to the proper point, and she therefore informed him that the
                     hallowed spot of ground which sustained her angelic frames might be found in a
                     cross-street, of respectable pretensions, which branched off from Broadway. She
                     intended returning to her home on that day, and in the evening would have the
                     happiness of receiving him beneath her father's roof. He would not find her
                     entirely alone, for a young friend, who was in her confidence, had consented to
                     bear her company; but she would be discreet enough to employ herself with a
                     book, while her presence satisfied the scruples of her father on the score of
                     propriety:
               
               
                  CHAPTER VII.
                  Punctual to the appointment, Grandval found himself in front of a block of
                     houses, the lower floors of which were evidently used for stores, and he
                     concluded that the family he came to visit lived above. He had very particular
                     directions to look for a certain number, and on one side of the main door a
                     smaller one would be found, bearing an old-fashioned brass knocker. A few blows
                     on this would be the open sessame, and the lover speedily
                     find himself in the presence of his mistress.
                  He found the door, struck twice, but before a third blow
                     could fall, it suddenly unclosed, and a narrow alley paved with brick, and diml
                     lighted with gas, was before him. He looked to see by what agency the opening
                     had been so quickly achieved, and saw a small boy with a frightened expression
                     behind the door, staring at him with all his might.
                  "Which way am I to go?" he asked of the urchin.
                  He pointed toward the dim obscurity in the distance, and said,
                  "Up stairs, please sir. The Gov'ner's done gone out, and Miss Mary is in the
                     front room with Miss Sally Muggridge, a waitin' for you"
                  "Good Heavens!" mentally exclaimed our hero, "can my refined and intellectual
                     Mary have a friend with such a name? Shakspeare need not say a rose by any
                     other name would smell as sweet; for if Miss Muggridge were as beautiful as
                     Helen, she would still be repulsive to one of fastidious taste, on account of
                     that odious patronymic."
                  These reflections were made as he stumbled up a stairway, on which the dim
                     gas-light only served to make darkness visible, while the whole atmosphere was
                     poisoned by its exhalations. He reached the upper floor in safety, and saw a
                     shaded lamp through an open door from which at the same time came a suppressed
                     giggle.
