                  A little disconcerted, he hesitated to advance, until a strange voice said,
                  "Walk in Mr. Grandval. My friend will receive you in a few moments. She is
                     slightly discomposed, and has retired for a little while. Pray come in."
                  As this address was made, the round fat figure of a very dumpy girl, with a
                     profusion of red hair, hanging in corkscrew ringlets over her rosy cheeks
                     appeared in the doorway.
                  She returned his elaborate bow by a little nod, and stepping backward made way
                     for him to enter the apartment. It was feebly lighted, but the extreme
                     dinginess of every thing in it was not to be concealed even by that means. A
                     dark colored carpet covered the floor, which dust and hard usage had all
                        JOHN SMITH, THE FOUNDER OF THE SMITH FAMILY.
                     
                      most bereft of any distinct
                     pattern—the tables were nearly concealed by woollen draperies which seemed to
                     have been chosen of as dull a color as possible, that their neutral tints might
                     not betray the want of tidiness, and on them lay a few books, which evidently
                     had seen hard service. Heavy curtains still draped the
                     windows, probably to catch the dust from the street without; for they looked as
                     if a touch would bury one beneath an avalanche of the material of which we may
                     be made, but from which we instinctively recoil in its primitive state.
                  Grandval was fastidiously neat; his early years had been spent in his light and
                     beautiful southern home, and the vision of elegance blended with taste, which
                     rose up before his memory, had unconsciously become his standard, and rendered
                     him observant of strange little things which those of his sex do not often
                     heed.
                  Miss Muggridge poured forth excuses and reasons for her friend's nervousness
                     with extreme fluency, while he looked around him with a feeling of discomfort
                     he was ashamed of. He endeavored to make excuses for the appearance of things,
                     by reflecting that Mr. Smith was evidently not rich, and he lived in a wretched
                     street to retain any appearance of neatness, for it was a constant
                     thoroughfare, and it must be impossible to keep the house in respectable order.
                     The most particular people would become discouraged after awhile, and suffer
                     the dust to have its own way.
                  He thus reasoned himself into a more complacent state of feeling, and after
                     uttering a vast quantity of words, which might as well have been Choctaw for
                     any comprehension our hero had of them, the good-humored little chatterbox
                     vanished through a side door, with the avowed object of bringing back her dear,
                     darling, nervous Mary.
                  Some moments elapsed before the door again unclosed, and Grandval was beginning
                     to become impatient for the meeting so unnecessarily delayed. It did open at
                     last, and disclosed the fat figure of Miss Muggridge, leading in a willowy
                     drooping form, which was as remarkable for thinness as her own was for embonpoint.
                  
                  Grandval started up in irrepressible consternation, for this time
                     he had believed it impossible that any mistake could have been made; and the
                     thought flashed upon him that he had actually committed himself in writing to
                     this lackadaisical girl, with a large nose, a slight squint, and a complexion
                     as untransparent as beeswax.
                  "O," groaned the wretched youth, "this caps the climax of all my
                     misadventures!"
                  He stood uncertain how to act, or what to say, a most pitiable object of
                     bewilderment, until Miss Smith advanced quite close to him, and held out a hard
                     long hand, while a smile gleamed on her thin lips. She spoke in a voice that
                     was any thing but musical,
                  "Dearest Mortimer, for as such I feel myself privileged to address you, I bid
                     you welcome to my father's house. How my heart has yearned for this meeting I
                     can never express to you, but I am now happy, O, blissfully happy in your
                     presence."
                  Grandval listened to this address in an agony of feeling language can not
                     depict, and he could have gnashed his teeth in rage at the terrible blunder he
                     had committed. He at length faltered.
                  "Do—do you really believe that we have met before, Miss Smith?"
                  "Can I doubt it? Your graceful form, your beaming eyes, your soul-stirring
                     voice, all made an impression upon me that time can never erase. I admired you
                     then, but your charming letters have completed the conquest of a heart
                     overflowing with sensibility."
                  "O, yes," chimed in Miss Muggridge, "my sweet Mary is all sentiment and
                     sensibility. She will render you the happiest and most enviable of men, Mr.
                     Grandval."
                  "Good God, this is too dreadful!" exclaimed Grandval, goaded beyond endurance.
                     "I—I—really, ladies, you must pardon me, but my own memory is to treacherous
                     that—that I have not the slightest recollection of ever meeting either of you
                     before. I am sorry to say so, Miss Smith, but a very singular mistake has
                     occurred, and my letters have been received by the wrong person."
                  Miss Smith shrieked, uttered a slight exclamation, and fainted. The bewildered
                     Grandval stood a mere spectator, while the fat little friend, with much ado,
                     supported the rigid figure to a chair, placed her on it, and as she made not
                     the slightest effort to assist herself, her feet rather ungracefully scraped
                     the floor, while her long arms hung down in two straight lines, the ends of her
                     fingers nearly touching the carpet.
                  Little Miss Muggridge then drew several deep breaths, which sounded very like
                     the sighs of a whale, and violently fanned herself and her insensible friend
                     with a bunch of ostrich feathers, such as are commonly used as dusters, but
                     whose office in that house must have been a sinecure. When she gained breath
                     enough to speak, she said:—
                  "I really think you are the strangest man I ever saw—to stand there like a
                     statue, and never offer to help me get this darling sensitive creature in a
                     safe position, when you know, too, you have reduced her to this condition."
                  Thus admonished Grandval aroused himself from his stupor, and looked around to
                     see if any thing could be found which might be of use in restoring an
                     insensible lady. A boquet of faded flowers stood on a table, and he thought the
                     water in which they were immersed might be beneficial, as a tiny shower bath;
                     he hurried forward, and seizing them, found that the vase was empty, and the
                     flowers them selves artificial. They were in his hands, and with a sort of
                     desperation he approached Miss Smith and held them beneath her nose.
                  However novel, it proved an efficient remedy, for she inhaled the dust with
                     which long exposure had covered them, and it produced such a violent fit of
                     sneezing, that the young lady started to her feet, and stamped them upon the
                     floor in the vexation of her spirit. It was contrary to the rules of all
                     romance, to have a scene so effectively got up, spoiled in so absurd a manner;
                     for, who ever before heard of a heroine recovered from a swoon by the
                     application of vulgar dust to her ol-factories?
                  Our hero certainly viewed its effects with consternation, for Miss Smith's pale
                     grey eyes sparkled, her brown complexion actually grew red, and she turned on
                     her friend reproachfully, and said:—
                  "Why did you allow him to treat me thus? O, false friend! I believe you wished
                     to render me ridiculous in his sight."
                  Miss Muggridge was justly offended at this charge, she threw up her head, and
                     shook her curls scornfully:—
                  "I need not attempt what you can do so much more effectually for
                     yourself, my dear. I dare say, Mr. Grandval thinks the recovery the least
                     absurd portion of the scene."
                  "O, my heart is wounded unto death," murmured Miss Smith, leaning her head upon
                     her hand, and weeping. "The man who has won my affections by his insidious
                     flatteries proves perfidious, and the friend of my soul treats me with
                     unfeeling levity. I feel as deserted as Ariadne when she wandered on the shores
                     of her lovely island, bewailing the loss of her Theseus. Mine, alas! is a worse
                     fate, for I seem to have lost both lover and friend."
                  The tender-hearted Muggridge could not stand this reproach: she drew near to
                     her, and placing her arm affectionately over her shoulder, said:—
                  "Forgive me, Mary; you know that I love and admire you, and it was you who
                     uttered the first taunt. To prove my sincerity, I will endeavor to bring this
                     gentleman to a sense of what is due to you. Are you certain, Mr. Grandval, that
                     the unfortunate mistake which has occurred, can not yet be made conducive to
                     your happiness and that of my friend? It seems to me an intervention of
                     Providence, to point out to you the plain road to future bliss."
                  "I cannot agree with you, Miss Muggridge" replied our hero, who began to
                     recover his self-possession. "Neither can I imagine how Miss Smith remembers
                     me, when I have not the slightest recollection of ever seeing her before, and
                     she certainly is an entirely different person from the one to whom my letters
                     really were addressed."
                  Miss Smith uttered little shrieks at this assertion, and seemed on the point of
                     fainting again; but as she was uncertain what other ridiculous remedy might be
                     sought for by her visitor, she thought better of it.
                  Miss Muggridge turned to her, and said:—
                  "You hear what he asserts, my dear. Tell him when you met him; recall the
                        whole scene to his mind, and
                     perhaps you may convince him that you have not played a part to entrap him into
                     this visit."
                  Miss Smith, thus adjured, assumed a more perpendicular position, and, fixing
                     her tearful eyes on Grandval, asked:—
                  "Did I not see you on the Empire State, in the month of July?"
                  Grandval started and looked more keenly at her; but in vain—he could not
                     remember her presence there.
                  "I certainly did go as far as Albany on that boat, in the month referred
                     to."
                  "And, and, do you not recall the fact that we sat opposite to each other at
                     dinner? There was a small hump-backed woman with me, whom you afterward
                     assisted through the crowd, and for whom you found a seat on deck, after we had
                     dined?"
                  "I remember that incident distinctly, but pardon me when I say that I scarcely
                     noticed her companion, and should never have recognized her in yourself. I saw
                     that the little woman had no protector, and she was pushed about in the crowd;
                     I extricated her from a simple impulse of humanity, and had no idea such an
                     adventure as the present could grow out of an act of ordinary politeness."
                  Miss Smith sighed, and looked very downcast at this unflattering explanation.
                     She said:—
                  "You will at least admit that when I received your letters, I was not to blame
                     in retaining them and believing they were addressed to myself?"
                  Grandval bowed, and replied:—
                  "It was unfortunate, but I can not attach any blame to you, Miss Smith."
                  "Then," exclamed Miss Muggridge, impetuously, "I see no reason why you shal not
                     make good your professions of devotion. As a gentleman, I certainly consider
                     your honor as pledged, for my friend's affections are irrevocably engaged, and
                     I dread for her the consequences of a refusal on your part to fulfill the
                     contract your letters will clearly prove."
                  Grandval winced—he replied:—
                  "I am sure your friend has too much pride of character to claim a hand offered
                     under such a misapprehension. The lady to whom those letters were addressed, I
                     met while descending the Hudson; and to prove to you my sincerity, I will show
                     you her picture, which I accidentally discovered in a Daguerrian gallery a few
                     days since, and succeeded in purchasing."
                  He drew forth the image of the true Mary Smith and held it up. Miss Muggridge
                     eagerly scanned the fair lineaments, but her friend, after taking one glance,
                     shut her eyes as tightly as if she never intended to open them again, and
                     hysterically bade them to take it away.
                  Grandval hastened to conceal it next to his troubled heart, and then ventured
                     to say,
                  "As those letters fell into your hands through a mistake, Miss Smith, I trust
                     you will not consider me impertinent if I request you to restore them to me
                     before I leave you. I pledge you my honor, your own shall be placed in your
                     hands at as early an hour as possible on to-morrow."
                  At this, the disappointed fair one grew decidedly hysterical, and she muttered
                     amid such singular contortions as were far from improving her appearance.
                  "No—no—no. I can not relinquish those precious souveniers of the most thrilling
                     event of my whole existence."
                  "But what value can they possess, when you know they were addressed to another
                     person?" remonstrated the young man.
                  "O, I can read them—weep over them, dream over them, and delude myself into the
                     belief that he who penned them was sincere in his professions. That he would
                     not willingly break a heart he has taught to adore him."
                  To this outburst, Grandval knew not what to reply; but Miss Muggridge thought
                     it the right time to attempt to touch his sensibilities in favor of her
                     afflicted friend. She said,
                  "You must be aware, Mr. Grandval, that the professions in those letters you are
                     so anxious to regain, would entitle my friend to damages before any court. My
                     Pa is a lawyer, and I know something of breach of promise cases. This would
                     make a fine one, I assure you. Had you not better make up your mind to accept
                     my dear Mary as your bride, and save all the trouble that may ensue? I can tell
                     you Mr. Smith is not a man to permit his daughter's affections to be trifled
                     with for nothing."
                  "O," said Grandval, haughtily, "if your friend wishes to make this untoward
                     affair a matter of speculation, I am quite ready to pay any reasonable sum for
                     the possession of my letters."
                  "O, Sally, Sally, how could you be so barbarous as to insinuate mercenary
                     motives to him whom you know I love so entirely, that I have not a thought or
                     wish beyond the possession of his affections," sobbed Miss Smith. "Go—go, false
                     man. I pardon you your perfidy, and when the sod rests upon my broken heart,
                     you may be moved to too late remorse for your inhuman conduct."
               
               
                  CHAPTER VIII.
                  Glad to be dismissed, Grandval arose to leave. He heard Miss Muggridge say to
                     her friend,
                  "Your Pa will not allow the affair to end thus, my love. Cheer up, I hear him
                     coming up the stairway now."
                  In fact, a heavy step was heard, and before our hero could effect his escape
                     from the room, the doorway was filled up by a figure that looked more like a
                     wine cask set on a pair of slender understandings than any thing else. A red,
                     burly face, immense nose, and a shining bald head were the most prominent of
                     Mr. Smith's attractions.
                  As if his own house did not furnish a sufficient quantity of dust for his
                     consumption, he carried a box in his hand, from which he took an immense pinch
                     of snuff every few seconds, and liberally dispensed it over his face and shirt
                     bosom.
                  He surveyed the party in some surprise, and then said,
                  "Hoity-toity, what's to pay here? Weeping and wailing Moll, when I expected
                     that by this time you had reg'larly appointed the day. Good evening, young man;
                     I hope you are not the cause of those tears, because I do not allow any body
                     but myself the privilege of making my girl cry."
                  "An unfortunate misunderstanding which I deeply regret, has happened, Mr.
                     Smith," said Grandval, with as much dignity as he could assume. "Unfortunately,
                     it is now too late to remedy it; so with your permission I will take my
                     leave."
                  "Not so fast, if you please, my young friend, Before you leave, I wish to
                     comprehend the nature of this misunderstanding, and perhaps I may be able to
                     set it right. I know all that has gone before, for my daughter has no
                     concealments from me; and as you seemed to be serious in your offer, I allowed
                     her to receive you to-night in company with her young friend here. How is it,
                     Sally? Your tongue is glib enough, and you will have to explain, for Moll is in
                     one of her die-away humors; and this youngster seems struck dumb by my
                     appearance."
                  With great volubility, Miss Muggridge gave her own version of the story, which
                     certainly placed our hero in no very enviable position in the eyes of the
                     father. When she concluded, Grandval said,
                  "It is useless, Mr. Smith, for me to give you my views of this affair. I will
                     only say to you that your daughter is not the lady to whom my
                     letters were addressed, and I demand their immediate restoration."
                  "Softly—softly, young gentleman. Such missives are sometimes valuable in more
                     senses than one. A lover's sighs may be made useful to raise the wind; the
                     fragments of a broken heart may serve to repair a shattered fortune, eh? Don't
                     you comprehend? You have a choice between matrimony or a matter of money, ha!
                     ha!"
                  Grandval replied, indignantly,
                  "I have already informed these young ladies that I will purchase them, if that
                     is what is desired. Pledge yourself to place them in my hands to-morrow, and in
                     return receive a fair equivalent in money, together with those of your
                     daughter, and I shall consider myself well freed from a most annoying
                     position."
                  Mr. Smith took a huge pinch of snuff, and after a pause, said,
                  "I will take a night to consider of your proposal, and my decision shall be made known to you to-morrow. Good evening
                     sir."
                  As nothing further was to be gained, Grandval made a general bow, stumbled
                     through the obscurity of the passage, nearly fell over the young porter who sat
                     on the stairs asleep, and found his way into the open air, with a feeling of
                     having escaped a fate he shuddered to contemplate
                  He made an irrevocable vow that nothing should tempt him to make another effort
                     to find the girl of his heart; and on reaching his room, he heroically removed
                     the beloved image from his person, and plunged it into the deepest recesses of
                     his trunk, where its attractions could no longer tempt him to commit another
                     folly in behalf of the original.
                  On the following morning, at an early hour, he was waited on by a gentleman of
                     portly dimensions, with a crop of very red hair, whom he had no difficulty in
                     identifying as the father of Miss Muggridge, even before he announced his
                     name.
                  He entered in quite a bustle, rubbed his hands together, as if very pleasant
                     thoughts were in his mind, and after introducing himself, said:—
                  "Happy to make your acquaintance, Mr. Grandval, though unfortunately it's not
                     in the most agreeable manner that could be thought of. All in a professional
                     way, however."
                  Grandval politely thanked him, and said he supposed Mr. Muggridge came on
                     behalf of the friends of his daughter, with whom he had become so unpleasantly
                     entangled.
                  "Exactly so, my dear sir; and I trust that after a night spent in meditation on
                     the blighted hopes and withered affections of my charming client, you have
                     concluded to be just to her and generous to yourself, in appropriating the hand
                     and heart of one of the most amiable and most excellent of girls. I have known
                     Miss Smith, sir, for years, and she is a paragon—a paragon, I assure you, and
                     might with justice be set up as a pattern to the rest of the sex."
                  "I do not dispute Miss Smith's excellent qualities, Mr. Muggridge," replied our
                     hero. "I am willing to concede to her every thing you claim, except my freedom.
                     I made it perfectly clear last evening, that I had no intention to marry this
                     young lady. The blunder I have already made is sufficiently mortifying without
                     consummating my own folly by giving my hand to a woman for whom I have not the
                     slightest preference. I hope you are the bearer of my letters."
                  "Why—ye-e-s—they certainly are in my possession," replied Mr. Muggridge
                     deliberatively; "but I cannot surrender them except on conditions."
                  "Name them, sir," said Grandval impatiently.
                  "In fact, the party interested has no objection to bring a suit which must
                     result in heavy damages."
                  He kept his eye fixed on the young man to watch the effect of his words. He
                     smiled, and scornfully said:—
                  "Which simply means that I must bid high to prevent those silly effusions from
                     coming before a jury, and covering me with ridicule. Name your price at once,
                     sir; circumlocution will be of no avail, for my mind is made up.
                  Mr. Muggridge sighed—
                  "I regret it deeply, my young friend, if such you will allow me to call you on
                     so brief an acquaintance. But really, now, is there no chance to induce you to
                     heal the wounded heart of this young lady?"
                  "None whatever, except with a golden salve," replied the inexorable Grandval,
                     decisively. "Again I say, name your terms."
                  Mr. Muggridge assumed a calculating air—
                  "You informed Miss Smith yourself, that you are a young gentleman of
                     independent fortune; in case a suit is brought, the damages will be laid in
                     proportion to your estate, say ten thousand, at the most moderate
                     calculation—perhaps twenty thousand. The jury always levy a heavy contribution
                     on the pockets of faithless lovers; which is quite right, in my opinion. Let me
                     see—the actual award would probably be from three to five thousand dollars,
                     besides the expense of going to law. Taking all things into consideration, I do
                     not think a cool thousand too much to pay to get out of the scrape."
                  "It is too much by half," replied Grandval. "I am willing to give five hundred,
                     and consider that as paying dearly for my folly; but I will give it, to be
                     saved all further annoyance."
                  "But consider, sir, you disappoint the well grounded expectation this young
                     lady entertained, of sharing your prosperity; and then you offer her in
                     exchange for the priceless gift of her affections, the paltry sum of five
                     hundred dollars."
                  "I have considered, Mr. Muggridge, and the disappointment for which money can
                     purchase a panacea, is not very likely to affect her happiness long. Therefore
                     I consider five hundred as much as it is worth; it is, at least, as much as I
                     will give; and this is my ultimatum."
                  This was decisive, and after a few more feeble attempts to obtain better terms,
                     the letters were exchanged, and Mr. Muggridge departed with an order on Drew
                     & Co. for five hundred dollars, payable at sight.
               
               
                  CHAPTER IX.
                  SEVERAL weeks rolled by, and our hero had not once ventured to take a peep at
                     the concealed miniature, for he dreaded that a sight of it would renew his
                     restless desire to discover the original.
                  About this time Medler ventured to return from the country, and soon made
                     overtures of reconciliation to his former dupe. Grandval, however, resented his
                     conduct too much to accept them, and that gentleman forthwith determined to
                     play some trick on him that should avenge his coldness. As his mind was fertile
                     in expedients, he soon resolved on his course.
                  The suggestion he had once made to Grandval, to call a convention of all the
                     Smiths, occurred to him, and he resolved to act on it without consulting the
                     person most interested in its success. He forthwith sent an advertisement to a
                     daily paper, which had a very wide circulation, headed thus:
                  
                     "AN ESTATE WANTING AN HEIR—An eccentric humorist, lately deceased, left a
                        considerable property in one of the southern states to a certain John Smith,
                        a resident of New York, with whom he formed an acquaintance while traveling.
                        The said John Smith having extended some ordinary civility to him, caused
                        him to be remembered, and he bequeathed the whole of his
                        fortune to him. An agent is now in the city who has the means of identifying
                        the true heir, and he will be glad to receive the gentleman of that name on
                        Thursday morning, at nine o'clock. His address is M. G., No. —, ——
                        street."
                  
                  Great was the commotion produced among the John Smiths, and they all began to
                     recall his traveling adventures, in the hope of being able to remember the
                     generous acquaintance who so handsomely rewarded ordinary civilities. Each one
                     was convinced in his own mind that he was the true heir, and many joyful hearts
                     went on their way to Mrs. Wilson's boarding-house on the appointed morning.
                  All the boarders had not breakfasted, and the man whose double duty it was to
                     attend the door and wait on the table was kept in a state of distraction by the
                     repeated demands upon him from both quarters.
                  On the first application, he was about to turn the visitor
                     away, with the assurance that no Mr. Magee lived there—for thus he understood
                     the initials given—but Medler had purposely placed himself in the way, and
                     explained to the waiter that M. G. meant Mr. Mortimer Grandval, who he knew had
                     made an appointment with some friends for that morning.
                  A message was forthwith dispatched to Mr. Grandval, who had not yet risen, and
                     before his toilette was completed, no less than twenty gentlemen, of all ages
                     and all sizes, were in the parlor, regarding each other with a most telligerent
                     expression—for each one intuitively felt that the others had come to claim the
                     inheritance to which he considered himself indubitably entitled.
                  At length the waiter rushed into Grandval's room in such a state of aggravation
                     that he could no longer remember the respect due to a gentleman of his
                     liberality, and exclaimed:
                  "For God's sake, Mr. Grandval, come down stairs, for every body in town is
                     inquiring after you. The front room is full of—I was going to say gentlemen,
                     but some of 'em don't seem to be that; but most of 'em are well
                     enough in looks to pass for it."
                  
                  Grandval stared.
                  "And what interest have I in these people?" he asked.
                  "I don't know, I'm sure, sir; but they all came to see you, and if you don't
                     come down I shall lose my senses directly; for they keep a coming, and them
                     that's there keep ringing the parlor bell to know when you will come down."
                  "This is very extraordinary; and did none of them send up their names?"
                  "They seemed to think you'd know all about 'em, sir, and it was of no use; but
                     two or three of our gentlemen came along and happened to know some of 'em, and
                     they called 'em all Smith. As sure as I'm living, I believe every man down
                     there is named Smith."
                  Our hero started, and grew pale. He felt certain that a trick had been played
                     on him by some one who was aware of his mad search after Mary Smith, but he had
                     spirit enough to meet the Smith family assembled below without flinching.
                  It occurred to him that he might even turn it to his own account, and he drew
                     forth the miniature of his beloved, and placing it in his pocket, descended to
                     the parlor. He paused a moment at the door, to contemplate the eager faces and
                     expectant air of the crowd within.
                  "Good morning, gentlemen," he affably said. "I feel myself highly honored by
                     having so many good citizens to make me a call; and if I lived in your midst, I
                     should expect that you came to solicit me to permit myself to be nominated as
                     your next Governor, or at least as a candidate for Congress. Permit me,
                     gentlemen, to inquire the cause of your appearance."
                  Thereupon arose a chorus of voices, amid which he could only distinguish the
                     words, "advertisement," "fortune," "heirs," "John Smith," etc.
                  "Excuse me, gentlemen; but if you will favor me by allowing one among you to
                     act as speaker, I shall the sooner be enabled to understand why you have
                     honored me with your presence."
                  Thereupon a well-grown specimen of the race of John Smiths, who towered to the
                     height of six feet two, drew a paper from his pocket, and, brandishing it above
                     his head; pointed to the advertisement.
                  "This is our authority for seeking you," said he, in a voice worthy of his
                     frame, "and it appears to me very singular that you were not expecting
                        me, at least, for I am certain I am the person referred
                     to."
                  "What gives you that certainty?" inquired many voices, "for I, and
                        I, and I, am the rightful heir," was chorussed
                     around him.
                  "Grandval waited until there was a slight lull, and then said—
                  "Permit me, gentlemen, if you please, to look at the advertisement referred to,
                     for I am sure I am innocent of having any thing to do with it."
                  "Are you not M. G.? And are you not from the south?" was aked by Hercules
                     Smith.
                  Our hero reflected.
                  "My initials are certainly M. G., for my name is Mortimer Grandval, and I came
                     from the south."
                  By this time the paper had reached him, and glancing at the notice with so
                     inviting a head, he said to the expectant crowd—
                  "I am sorry to inform you, gentlemen, that this is a hoax; and one I assure
                     you, on my honor, I am entirely innocent of participating in. I believe the
                     malice to be leveled at myself, but to that I am indifferent. I only regret
                     that your expectations should have been so shamefully raised only to be
                     disappointed."
                  There was an air of sincerity about him which, together with his graceful and
                     gentlemanly bearing, impressed the John Smiths favorably toward him, and
                     several shook hands with him on the spot, and hoped that this would not be
                     their last meeting. He returned them his thanks, and then said—
                  "Since you have assembled here, gentlemen, I will inquire if there is one among
                     you who remembers seeing me on an Albany steamer in July last? I had then the
                     pleasure of meeting with a gentleman of your somewhat extensive family, who was
                     in company, with his wife and daughter. I should be extremely happy to renew my
                     acquaintance with him."
                  "Bless my soul! is it possible?" said a voice from the farthest corner of the
                     room. "I—I had nearly forgotten the circumstance, or rather I was heedless, for
                     now I remember you perfectly. Excuse me, Mr. Grandval; I am in affliction, and
                     only accompanied a friend here who hoped to be benefitted, for I do not myself
                     reside in New York."
                  As he spoke, a middle-sized, gentlemanly-looking man came forward, looking
                     thinner and more care-worn than when our hero had last seen him; but he
                     instantly recalled him to his recollection.
                  "I am delighted to meet with you, Mr. Smith, I do assure you. Pray how are your
                     excellent lady and charming daughter?"
                  At the mention of his daughter, the father's face became gloomy, and Grandval
                     felt himself grow pale.
                  "Mrs. Smith is quite well, thank you, but I am sorry to say that the failing
                     state of Mary's health brings me to the city."
                  "Nothing serious, I hope?" gasped the agitated listener, nearly breathless with
                     emotion.
                  "I am afraid it is. She is now confined to her bed the greater part of the
                     time, and her disease seems to baffle the skill of her physicians."
                  To find her but to lose her, by the stern decree of death, was too terrible,
                     and Grandval showed such manifest emotion that the crowd of Smiths had
                     sufficient delicacy to depart, and leave him with his newly found friend.
                  "Mr. Smith," said he, mournfully, "I will not conceal from you that your
                     daughter made an indelible impression upon me, and I have spent my time, since
                     I lost sight of you, in endeavoring to trace you in this city, in the belief
                     that you resided here."
                  "No, my dear young friend, I live in Geneva. We left for our own home on the
                     same day we parted from you, but my wife and daughter have not forgotten their
                     pleasant steamboat acquaintance; they have frequently mentioned you since. Mary
                     must have taken cold from imprudent exposure on that night, for she has never
                     been well since. Five weeks ago we came to New York to consult Dr. ——, and I
                     now remember that my daughter said that she had seen you when out riding. Since
                     that evening she has steadily grown worse, and I greatly fear that her days are
                     numbered."
                  Grandval was overwhelmed. In the anguish of his spirit he related the history
                     of his quest to the afflicted father, and declared that if Mary perished, the
                     light of his life would be extinguished.
                  After a conversation of some length the two parted. Mr. Smith gave his address
                     to Grandval, and invited him to visit him very soon, when he hoped that better
                     news of his daughter's state might he imparted to him.
               
               
                  CHAPTER X.
                  Grandval wrote down the address, and carried it to his room. He placed it on a
                     table, where he could see it at every turn in his troubled walk across the
                     floor, and gave way to the emotions of mingled joy and fear that filled his
                     mind. He would not believe that he had found her only to be parted irrevocably
                     from her; she would yet recover, and be won by such true love as he could
                     offer.
                  In the midst of his reflections he was summoned to breakfast, and leaving the
                     card upon the table, he descended to the discussion of a cup of coffee and a
                     hot muffin, for which delicacies he had very little appetite, for the sad news
                     of Mary's illness almost counterbalanced his satisfaction in having at last
                     discovered her.
                  Medler, hoping to witness a rare scene, had stationed himself in the room next
                     to the parlor, from which he could be an unseen witness of all that passed in
                     the interview between our hero and the Smiths. He was greatly disappointed when
                     he saw how well Grandval sustained himself in so embarrassing a
                     predicament.
                  He was vexed and surprised to find that the long sought one proved to be among
                     them; and listened eagerly to the conversation which ensued between the
                     two.
                  He still resented Grandval's indifference to the charms of his fair cousin, and
                     his refusal to receive his own overtures for a reconciliation, still further
                     exasperated him; until he was resolved to repay him in such a manner as should
                     inflict as much annoyance on him as he had himself endured.
                  
                  While meditating on the most feasible plan to pursue, he passed Grandval's
                     room; the door was half open and looking in, he saw that it was unoccupied. In
                     a comprehensive glance around, his eye 
                        ligthed
                        lighted
                      on the card of address; in an instant it was in his hand, and a new
                     scheme in rapid process of formation in his active brain.
                  The address was written with a pencil, and the number thirteen on it, was
                     easily changed to fifteen by a light dash over the last figure, which was very
                     carelessly made. Believing that Grandval would much sooner trust to the
                     evidence of the numbers on the card than to his own memory, he laid it down in
                     great elation, and hastened to his own room.
                  In the afternoon, just as our hero was preparing to call on Mr. Smith for the
                     purpose of inquiring after the fair invalid, an urgent message came from Mr.
                     Drew, which demanded his immediate presence at the residence of that gentleman.
                     He hurried thither at once, and after some circumlocution, was informed by him
                     of the severe illness of his uncle, which threatened to terminate fatally. A
                     telegraphic dispatch had been forwarded to Mr. Drew, requesting him to
                     communicate immediately with Grandval, and inform him that by departing as soon
                     as possible, he might be enabled to reach home before the fatal event.
                  The young man was deeply affected, for his uncle was the only near relative he
                     possessed, and he was sincerely attached to him. To make arrangements for
                     immediate departure was necessary, and together the two gentlemen went out to
                     ascertain what steamers were up for the port of Charleston, and to engage a
                     berth without delay.
                  Grandval returned at a late hour to Mrs. Wilson's, after completing every
                     preparation for his departure on the following evening at six o'clock.
                  "I will seek Mr. Smith to-morrow," he thought, and inform him of the reasons
                     for my sudden departure. I will endeavor to induce him to write me an account
                     of Mary's convalescence, for I am certain she must recover."
                  With this thought he fell into a troubled slumber, which was interrupted by
                     terrific dreams. He awoke unrefreshed, and in a state of great depression
                     wended his way to the counting house of Drew & Co., where business
                     imperatively demanded his presence.
                  When he was released it was to late to call on Mr. Smith, for his lodgings were
                     in quite a different portion of the city from his own.
                  At four o'clock in the afternoon, he entered Brian's cab, and again set forth
                     with that worthy in his old search.
                  "Is it after them Smiths, again, Mr. Grandval?" he asked, "I hope ye knows this
                     time where to go to, for by my conscience I think that last scrape at the
                     pollis office ought to giv' ye a lastin' disgust to the whole of the
                     family."
                  "I have the address this time, Brian, so there can be no mistake. Drive on
                     quickly to number 15 —— street."
                  Brian touhced his horse, and rapidly passed streets and houses, until they came
                     to the required one. As they drove toward the number they sought, Grandval's
                     heart sank within him; for there was a long array of carriages before the
                     house, headed by a hearse; and the cold conviction came to him that his Mary
                     was no more. A dimness came over his vision, and when the cab drew up in the
                     rear of the other carriages, he had recovered so far as to see that they were
                     bringing out a coffin covered with white, and a gentleman and lady he took to
                     be Mr. and Mrs. Smith followed it, and stepped into the vehicle nearest the
                     hearse.
                  "What shall we do next?" asked Brian in a low tone.
                  "You won't go a visitin' at that house this evenin' I
                     suppose?"
                  "Follow the proeession," replied Grandval feebly, "I can, at least, see her
                     laid in her grave."
                  "Blessed Lord! you don't say that the Miss Smith you've had such a time a
                     lookin' after, has gone and died just as you've found her?"
                  "I shall never find her now, until we meet in heaven, Brian. Say no more, my
                     good fellow, I can not bear it."
                  Brian looked very sympathetic, and when the slow march to the burying-ground
                     was finished, he made some inquiries of the bystanders about the deceased, and
                     the replies he received to them only served to confirm Grandval in his gloomy
                     error.
                  "The young lady had been sick some time, but her death was at last sudden and
                     the physicians had said it was caused by disease of the heart"
                  Grandval saw the remains of her he believed to be his Mary deposited in a
                     vault, and without attempting to speak to the afflicted parents, took his way
                     to the steamer in a state of terrible depression.
                  His voyage was a melancholy one, but some consolation awaited him at its close.
                     He found his beloved uncle convalescent, and in his gratitude for this mercy,
                     endeavored to reconcile himself to the loss of his first love.
                  Widowed in heart he considered himself, and he spent hours each day in
                     contemplating the image of his lost Mary, in the full belief that no other
                     could fill the place in his affections left vacant by her untimely death.
               
               
                  CHAPTER XI.
                  AS TIME waned, and the memory of his lost Mary became rather a pleasing
                     melancholy than an acute suffering, a strong desire sprang up in the heart of
                     Grandval to write to her parents, and express to them the tender feeling of
                     reverence and respect which filled his mind toward those who stood in so near a
                     relation to his lost love. We have said that he was romantic, and this desire
                     perhaps will prove it, but the wish to offer sincere sympathy to those who had
                     suffered so terrible a bereavement, also proved that he possessed warm and true
                     feeling.
                  His letter was naturally and gracefully expressed, and was dispatched as soon
                     as finished. He looked with much anxiety for a reply; it came in due course of
                     time, and the revulsion in his feelings be imagined when he read the following
                     words:
                  
                     
                        
                           
                              MY DEAR YOUNG FRIEND,—The reception of your letter filled me with
                                 pleasure, and also with astonishment. How so strange a
                                 misconception has occurred I can not imagine, but certain it is,
                                 that no such afflicting event as the loss of my beloved daughter
                                 has taken place; for she is sitting before me now, looking delicate
                                 it is true, but in no danger of a speedy departure to the land of
                                 shadows. For several days after our last meeting, we expected you
                                 to call, and I watched with some solicitude. I accidentally saw a
                                 list of the passengers on a southern steamer, and among them was
                                 your name. I supposed that you had been suddenly summoned home, and
                                 I must confess that I looked for a communication from you for many
                                 weeks. As none came, I concluded that you had relinquished your
                                 pursuit, just as it promised to terminate successfully.
                              Since you have so freely confessed your sentiments toward my
                                 daughter, I need feel no hesitation in saying to you that there is
                                 an inexplicable connection between her illness and her acquaintance
                                 with yourself. My observations have convinced me of this, though it
                                 seems strange that a brief association of a few hours should exert
                                 such an influence on even the most sensitive of natures.
                              When I returned home on the morning of our memorable interview, I
                                 related what had occurred, to my wife and daughter. The brightening
                                 eyes, and flushed cheeks with which Mary listened, gave me a clue
                                 to the cause of her listlessness, and failing health, which, I own,
                                 took me quite by surprise. From that hour there was a perceptible
                                 improvement in my daughter's health and spirits. Although you did
                                 not come as you promised, some internal voice seemed to whisper of
                                 hope and happiness in the future. She again went forth with the
                                 delicate bloom of renewed health upon her cheek, and a sweet light
                                 in her blue eyes, that seemed to speak of inward joy.
                              During the winter, this mental source of content sustained her
                                 spirits, but as spring opened, and we heard nothing further from
                                 you, she again drooped, and all my fears for her were renewed.
                                 Yesterday morning she sat before me with her white hands folded,
                                 and her clear eyes fixed on the distant clouds that floated over
                                 the azure sky; as I looked on her, I could not help fancying that
                                 she too would soon pass as a soft shade from amid those who so
                                 tenderly lover her. Letters were brought in, and among them I found yours. I read it and
                                 then placed it in her hands. She trembled, flushed, and grew pale
                                 alternately, and tears fell as she perused your lamentations over
                                 her supposed death. She covered her face with her thin hands for
                                 some moments, and seemed to pray fervently—she then looked up at me
                                 with the serene expression of a seraph, and said,
                              "I was wrong to lose my trust in the future, dear father. I have
                                 often said in my heart, 'he will surely come,' but of late I again
                                 doubted and was desolate."
                              I inquired,
                              "How can this be, my child? your acquaintance with Mr. Grandval was
                                 too brief to produce any permanent influence upon your fate?"
                              "I know not how it is," she gently rejoined, "I only know that when
                                 I lost sight of him, it seemed to me that the brightest hopes of my
                                 existence had departed with him. I became restless, hopeless, the
                                 sun no longer fell upon my pathway, and I was cold and desolate. It
                                 is a mystery to me, my father, but I simply tell you the
                                 truth."
                              This avowal I give to you, because your constancy, your sincere
                                 affection for my daughter, even when you believed that the portals
                                 of the grave had closed upon her, seem to demand it. Come then, and
                                 give me a son in one I know to be worthy of the affection he has
                                 inspired. Already Mary's face wears a brighter expression—her form
                                 seems to have lost that drooping lassitude which so deeply
                                 distressed us. She smiles, and the light of happiness is once more
                                 in her eyes.
                              
                                 Your sincere friend.
                                 JOHN SMITH.
                              
                           
                        
                     
                  
                  To paint the extatic joy which thrilled the soul of Grandval at the perusal of
                     this letter, would be impossible. He acted many extravagances, which in his
                     more sober hours he blushed to recall; but we shall not relate them, as we have
                     a sincere respect for him, and a warm interest in his happiness.
                  It was the latter part of May when the knowledge that Mary still lived was
                     conveyed to him, and he lost no time in preparing for a summer tour to the
                     north. At the same time he issued extensive orders for beautifying, and
                     refurnishing his house for the reception of his bride. Delighted with the
                     prospect of having a new niece, and one as charming as Grandval declared his
                     lady love to be, his uncle undertook to have every thing arranged to his
                     satisfaction by the time he had completed his bridal trip. With a heart beating
                     most joyfully, our hero journeyed toward Charleston, at which port he embarked
                     for New York.
                  On the day of his arrival in that city, it chanced that a new and magnificent
                     steamer was about to depart for Liverpool. As Grandval had never visited one of
                     the English lines, and he had a few hours of leisure be ore him, he determined
                     to go on board.
                  He was walking around the cabin, examining the embellishments, when suddenly he
                     felt his hand grasped, and turning, beheld the florid features of Mr.
                     Muggridge, beaming upon him as brightly as if ill-humor were a thing unknown to
                     him even by name.
                  "My dear sir, this is the happiest renconter," he said. "I was wishing only
                     yesterday, that you could know the astonishing results of that little business
                     transaction of ours. 'Pon, my honor, I am amazed when I think of it."
                  "I am glad to hear that some good has grown out of that stupid affair to one of
                     the parties concerned," said Grandval, pleasantly. "Pray, how is Miss
                     Smith?"
                  "Miss Smith no longer," replied Mr. Muggridge, pointing mysteriously toward the
                     ladies' cabin. "She is in there, about to depart on a tour to Europe with her
                     husband, Samuel Brown, junior, who was fortunate enough to be a clerk in the
                     firm to which advices were sent, and he was wise enough to offer himself before
                     the news of the golden shower reached either the lady or her father. He
                     happened to have some slight acquaintance with Miss Smith before, and it was no
                     difficult matter to persuade her that he was desperately smitten with her long
                     nose and sallow face; for between you and me, Miss Smith is'nt the handsomest
                     woman born since the days of Venus."
                  "Pray, explain, Mr Muggridge. What connection have I with this
                     good fortune of hers?"
                  "O, it all came from your five hundred. Her father was advised to take shares
                     in a California mining company; he invested the whole of it in that way; bought
                     shares at a discount from those who had become discouraged by the accounts from
                     Eldorado. Just then, the miners turned the bed of a river—found a bar that
                     yielded enormously, and the last steamship brought out the news that Miss
                     Smith's investment of five hundred had yielded about fifty thousand clear.
                     Lucky fellow, was'nt he? Don't you feel sorry now that Mr. Brown was before
                     you! If you had come back only a few weeks sooner, you might have stepped in
                     and won; for I believe she has a weakness toward you yet. Those sentimental
                     love-letters made a deep impression."
                  "Pooh! nonsense! I am happy to hear that the young lady has been so fortunate,
                     and no longer regret the sacrifice of the sum I paid her, since it has produced
                     such magnificent cent results. Pray, how is Miss Muggridge? I assure you, I
                     remember her with much more interest than her lackadaisical friend."
                  "Thank you. My daughter is also married to a young man in prosperous business—a
                     cousin of Miss Smith. My only objection was to his name, which is far more
                     common than my own."
                  "Tell her from me, if you please, Mr. Muggridge, never to wrong a son so deeply
                     as to bestow on him the name of John."
                  Mr. Muggridge laughed.
                  "I shall be sure to deliver your message. But pray, how speeds your own search?
                     Have you found the true owner of the name yet?"
                  "I have been so fortunate, and I am now on my way to claim her. This time there
                     is no mistake."
                  "Glad to hear it, my young friend; but here comes the bridal party."
                  The cabin doors unclosed, and a group of persons stood together, among whom
                     Grandval recognized Mrs. Brown, and the ci
                        devant Miss Muggridge. A small, insignificant looking man, whose
                     head barely reached her shoulder, stood beside his former correspondent while
                     the dumpy little woman looked as if she were standing on tiptoe to reach the
                     arm of an immensely tall personage to which she clung.
                  Grandval surveyed the party at a distance, and then excusing himself from a
                     nearer approach, bade adieu to Mr. Muggridge, and took his departure.
               
               
                  CHAPTER XII.
                  IT was night when our lover reached the charming little city of the lake; as
                     lovely a night as moonlight ever aided to make glorrious. It was too late to
                     visit Mr. Smith's family that evening, so our romantic young gentleman
                     concluded that a sail on the lake would pleasantly fill up the hours until he
                     felt disposed to sleep. Ah, little did he imagine that what he would there
                     behold would murder sleep effectually for him; unfortunate wight that he
                     was!
                  A boat was easily procured, and wrapped in a dreamy reverie, he lounged
                     indolently in the stern, listening to the measured dips of the oars as the
                     fairy craft glided over the silvery water, whose placid surface resembled
                     another concave heaven, sparkling with the gems of night.
                  Suddenly, long lines of earthly light shed their yellow radiance in tremulous
                     lines across this blue expanse, and looking up, Grandval saw that the windows
                     of a magnificent mansion had been thrown open, and the illuminated rooms were
                     reflected in the water. A party of gay young people seemed to occupy them, for
                     the murmur of conversation and musical laughter came in blended confusion upon
                     the summer air. While several groups promenaded on a terraced walk which
                     overhung the lake, two persons descended a flight of stone steps, the lower one
                     of which was washed by the waves; a small boat of elegant shape, and highly
                     ornamented, was moored to an iron ring; into this the gentleman assisted his
                     white-robed companion, placing his arm around her waist to steady her
                     movements, and murmuring words in her ear of which Grandval could well divine
                     the import. As a poisoned barb to his inmost soul was this scene, for in the
                     lithe figure, blonde curls, and fair face of the girl, he recognized her he had
                     believed devoted to himself, listening with downcast eye and blushing cheek to
                     the whispered words of love from another!
                  A light breeze was stirring, and after placing his companion in safety among
                     the cushions in the stern, the young man set a small sail, and throwing himself
                     at her feet, together they floated out upon those star-lit waves, oblivious of
                     all the world beside themselves Grandval saw her bend forward until her fair
                     curls almost touched his brow; beheld him clasp her white hand, and press it to
                     his lips; he could endure no more.
                  His own boat lay in the shadow flung by a large tree across the water, and at a
                     motion of his hand, the oarsman had stopped, thus permitting him to behold
                     himself the evidences of inconstancy on the part of her he had believed the
                     purest and loveliest of her sex. Bewildered, crushed, madly jealous, he gazed a
                     few moments on the receding pair, and then, in a stern tone, commanded the man
                     to return to the point from which they had started. This person had not before
                     spoken, but he now said,
                  "That is the bride elect. To-morrow morning Miss Smith marries the gentleman
                     who is in the boat with her."
                  "Marries him! so soon! Impossible!" exclaimed Grandval.
                  "Not so very soon. She saw him in Albany a year ago, and he followed her home;
                     but I believe it was only lately settled that she should marry him."
                  This was the severest blow of all! To be encouraged to hope; to believe that
                     the heart of his chosen one responded mysteriously to his own; to seek her in
                     the firm faith that a pure mutual love existed, and to find her on the eve of
                     marriage with another! It was too dreadful. She must have deceived her father
                     most shamefully, yet this want of truth in her did not lessen the bitter pang
                     which struck to his heart with the
                     conviction of her unworthiness.
                  He did not sleep that night; he made no effort to retire, but sat wretched, and
                     bowed down beside his window, and thought almost to madness upon the unexpected
                     close to his bright dreams of future happiness. He would ee her once more; he
                     would in person behold her consummate her treachery to himself by giving her
                     hand to another, and then abjuring the perfidious sex forever, would return to
                     the seclusion of his own plantation, follow the example of his uncle, and dwell
                     in single blessedness.
                  Grandval had ascertained from the boatman that the marriage was to take place
                     in the Episcopal Church in the town. At an early hour he betook himself toward
                     the edifice, and finding the doors unclosed, he entered and sought a seat so
                     far removed from the altar as to avoid observation, yet near enough to see all
                     that might transpire there.
                  Two wretched hours passed, when the silence was broken by the entrance of a
                     considerable party. First came the bride and groom, supported by four
                     attendants; her father and mother, with a number of friends, followed, and
                     crowded around the altar. Grandval saw but one figure among them, his lost
                     love, looking in her white robes and shadowy veil, more like some bright spirit
                     from another sphere, than a fair, frail, inconstant flesh and blood-being,
                     belonging to this."
                  How he steeped his soul in wretchedness as he listened to the words which gave
                     her to another! Until they were actually spoken he would not believe that such
                     a thing could be; even then, he almost fancied himself suffering under the
                     tortures of a terrible nightmare.
                  Grandval fixed one last despairing glance upon the features
                     which would never more smile for him, and was about to rush forth, when one of
                     the attendants approached and raised her face to imprint a kiss upon the cheek
                     of the bride. He stood as one electrified, and light, hope, joy, once more
                     streamed into his agitated heart. The two were so nearly alike, that but for
                     the slight dissimilarity in costume, it would have been impossible to
                     distinguish them apart. He knew at a glance that they must be twin sisters, and
                     she who acted as bridesmaid was his own Mary.
                  He sat down, overcome by the sudden revulsion of feeling, and when he recovered
                     himself, the pageant had passed out, and the Church was once more quiet.
                  
                  THAT evening, two persons were beside each other on the terrace which overhung
                     the lake. Grandval had at last overcome all difficulties, and stood beside his
                     fair traveling companion, with her hand clasped in his own, while he related
                     his adventures in search of herself, at which she laughed with a girlish
                     merriment it was delightful to listen to.
                  "And you really believed I had jilted you at last?" she said. "Pray, tell me
                     how you passed last night with this terrible conviction in your mind."
                  "As I hope never to pass another," replied Grandval, seriously. "I suffered
                     inexpressibly. Recal the desolation which, by your own confession, you once
                     have known, dear Mary; mingle with it in fancy, a fiery feeling of wrong and
                     jealousy, and you may have a faint conception of my sufferings. I would not
                     pass such another night for worlds."
                  "Your misconception was very natural. Augusta and myself are so nearly alike,
                     that as children we wore different ornaments to enable those around us to
                     distinguish us apart. When I met you, we had left her in Albany with an aunt,
                     with whom she remained until late in the fall. It was while with her that she
                     became acquainted with Mr. Kingston. But pray, what do you mean by my
                     desolation? I am sure I have not made any confession of the kind."
                  "Ah, Mary, seek not to deprive me of half the happiness of this hour by
                     practicing a coquetry which I am sure is foreign to your nature. You dreamed of
                     me, as I of you; and your spirit pined for the presence of its familiar. Was it
                     not thus?"
                  "Have it so, if it flatters you," she replied, with a smile and a blush.
                     "People with common sense would say that I took cold during our promenade on
                     that night of our first meeting; but as we are lovers, we are privileged to
                     talk nonsense; so you may cherish your own belief on the subject."
                  Within two weeks from that evening, Grandval's perseverance was rewarded by
                     receiving the hand of the fair Mary.
                  He has consented that his trials shall be published, as a warning to the Smith
                     family, never again to call a son John.
               
               THE END.
            
            
               BLANCHE DE BEAULIEU.
               BY DUMAS.
               
                  I.
                  WHOEVER, on the evening of the fifteenth of December, 1795, might have left the
                     little town of Clisson, to go to the village of Saint Crepin, and had stopped
                     upon the crest of the mountain at the foot of which runs the river la Moine,
                     would have seen a strange sight on the other side of the valley.
                  At first, in the spot where he would have looked for the village hidden among
                     the trees, in the midst of the horizon, already dusky in the twilight, he would
                     have perceived three or four columns of smoke, which, separate at their base,
                     joined in widening, floated a moment like a burnished dome, and, yielding
                     quietly to a damp west wind, rolled in that direction, lost in the clouds of a
                     lowering, hazy sky. He would have seen this base redden slowly, then all smoke
                     cease, and, in its place, forked tongues of flame burst from the roofs of the
                     houses, with a dull roaring, then twisting spirally, then again curving and
                     raising itself like the mast of a vessel. It would have appeared to him, that
                     soon after all the windows opened to belch forth fire. Occasionally, when a
                     roof fell in, he might have heard a dull sound, he might have seen a brighter
                     flame filled with thousands of sparks, and, by the red glare, of the increasing
                     conflagration, the shining of arms, a circle of soldiers extending afar. He
                     might have heard shouts, and bursts of laughter; and, he might have said in his
                     fright, "God pardon me! It is an army warming themselves by burning a
                     village."
                  In fact, a republician brigade of some twelve or fifteen hundred men had found
                     the village of Saint Crepin deserted, and had set it in flames.
                  This was not cruelty, but an expedient of war; a plan of the campaign, as any
                     other might have been; and the one which experience had proved to be the
                     best.
                  Nevertheless, a cottage standing by itself was not consumed; they appeared even
                     to take every necessary precaution to prevent the fire from reaching it. Two
                     sentinels guarded the entrance, and every moment orderly officers and
                     aids-de-camp went in and out carrying orders.
                  He, who issued these orders, was a young man; he appeared to be from twenty to
                     twenty-two years old; with long, light-colored hair, parted on his forehead,
                     which fell wavingly on each side of his pale and thin cheeks; his entire face
                     bore the impress of that fatal sadness which is to be seen upon the brow of
                     those destined to an early grave. His blue cloak, while enveloping him, did not
                     entirely conceal the badge of his rank, two General's epaulettes; though these
                     epaulettes were made of worsted—the Republican officers having made a patriotic
                     offering to the Convention of all the gold ornaments about their dress. He was
                     leaning upon a table, with a geographical chart spread out before him, on which
                     he was tracing in pencil, by the light of a lamp, which was itself eclipsed by
                     the glare of the conflagration, the route that his soldiers were about to
                     follow. It was General Marceau, who, three years later, was killed at
                     Altenkirchen.
                  "Alexandre!" called he, raising himself self half-way, "Alexandre! everlasting sleeper, are you dreaming of San
                     Domingo, that you sleep so long?"
                  "What's the matter?" inquired he to whom this was addressed, starting upright
                     in surprise, and whose head almost touched the ceiling of the cabin, "What's
                     the matter? is the enemy upon us?"—and these words were pronounced with that
                     light creole accent which preserved its softness, even in the midst of the
                     threatening tone.
                  "No, but an order has arrived for us from the commander-in-chief, General
                     Westermann."
                  And whilst his colleague read the order, for he to whom he had spoken was his
                     colleague, Marceau looked with childish curiosity upon the muscular form of the
                     herculean mulatto before him.
                  He was a man twenty-eight years old, with short, crisp hair, brown complexion,
                     high forehead and white teeth; whose almost supernatural strength was well
                     known to the army, which had seen him in a fight split a helmet down to the
                     cuirass, and once upon a parade strangle a fractious horse, which he was
                     riding, between his thighs. Nor had he long to live, but, less fortunate than
                     Marceau, he must die far from the battle field, poisoned by the order of a
                     king. This was General Alexandre Dumas—my father.
                  "Who brought this order?" asked he.
                  "The people's representative, Delmar."
                  "'Tis well, and where are these poor devils going to meet?"
                  "In a wood, a league and a half from here; look at the map, there it is."
                  "Yes; but on the map there are no ravines, no mountains, no tree stumps, no by
                     ways, embarassing the true route, where one can scarcely find one's self even
                     in the daytime. Infernal country! and withall that it is always so cold."
                  "Look!" said Marceau, pushing the door open with his foot and showing the
                     village on fire, "go out and warm yourself.—Ha! what is the matter,
                     citizens?"
                  This question was addressed to some soldiers, who, in seeking for provisions,
                     had discovered, in a sort of kennel attached to the cottage where the two
